RATING: 8
Great fun.  An instant hit with me.  Lots and lots going on in a very well put together package.  When Queen get it right (about 50% of the time) they normally turn out a really good game and this is in that bracket.  Not only that, but this game is typical of their artwork and the general quality of pieces and board - excellent.  Many of the mechanisms in this are huge fun and I think it's one that's going to get a lot of play over the years.  It seems hard to go for the Exhibitions, but aside from that I loved the flow of the game and the balancing mechanism of turn order decided by weeks taken to achieve things is really good.  It's great fun playing "feely bags in the desert" - now I know how my son feels playing Laura's Star!  Parts of the movement mechanic (balloons and trying to save time) reminded me a bit of Around the World in Eighty Days.  But there's much more going on in this, and it's ultimately a better game.  If you think there's "not enough control in this," stop bleating and go and play something else.  This game is great as it is!

RATING: 6

I wish there were a way to have the bag be less random, but even that ends up being fun and thematic.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Thebes is a very good push your luck game combined with route optimization and set collection. In the game we play the roles of archeologists excavating various regions of Europe and Asia in order to get prestige. The theme is very accurate in this game in all aspects, and I enjoyed it a lot.

It certainly have a lot of luck on the draw of artifacts in the excavation, but the luck can be mitigated well with more preparation and knowledge of the region you want to excavate.

There are some cool decisions you can make in this game, concentrate knowledge in one region, have sparse knowledge in all regions, focus on general knowledge, try to excavate early to have more chances of finding anything useful? spend a lot of weeks excavating to increase your chances of finding better artifacts, concentrating on expositions, concentrating on being the best in specific knowledge about all regions, etc.

I really liked the theme, the simplicity and the pace of this game, recommend to everyone.

RATING: 10
I've played both the original version and the Queen version of the game. The Queen version is very nice. Overall the game is unique and very thematic. I love the time track and how it affects play. You always have to juggle time in everything you do. The digging is also very well done as well. Sometimes even with the best information you just don't find anything. Exactly as it should be. Excellent family game. One of my 9 yr old daughters favorite games and the 7 year old always wants to play as well. Has everything a good family game should have; great theme, nice components, easy to learn rules, relatively short playing time, and some luck to ensure the same players don't win over and over.

------------------------------------------------------

Pretty much very simple, and with a theme that is appealing.  I absolutely get that it's highly luck-dependent, but it's fun enough not to be a problem.  You just can't get too caught up in the "I played better I should have won".  The time-turn-order mechanism is very clever and tends to result in little downtime.  I enjoyed the game, even if I find it easy to get discouraged by the draws.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Jenseits and Neuland seem to have (independently?) given birth to the same clever mechanic of actions taking varying amounts of time and position on a time track determining who takes the next turn.  This puts players under constant pressure to achieve as much as possible in a limited time and beat their opponents to the dig/exhibition/source of knowledge.  There's a delicious tension around how long you spend gathering knowledge at the risk of being beaten to the first shot at digging up some artifacts.  The other systems built around this clever central mechanic all work very smoothly.  The digging is nicely thematic but does bring a significant chance element.  I find the luck tolerable in a game that I simply enjoy playing.  Queen has done a very nice production job on its edition of this game.

RATING: 7.5
Ok. Take everything I say as completely biased... my first career was archaeology.  I've spent weeks in the field DOING archaeology.  Doing archaeology is a much more interesting experience than playing Thebes.  But that shouldn't be a surprise.  What I like about Thebes is the randomness of the artifact pulls.  That is reality in the field.  Sometimes you hit the jack-pot other times you get skunked.  The use of independent time passage for each player is also a very nice mechanic.  Overall, Thebes is not my first choice, but it is a good game from time to time.

RATING: 8
Finally got this after a quest worthy of Indiana Jones himself. Been out of stock absolutely everywhere but thought I'd have no prob at Games Expo. Found 1 copy at the show for 30 quid, quite expensive I thought but never mind. After my shift at the show was over, went to get it but it had already been snapped up. Curses! Asked at the Gameslore stall and they said they had a copy they could get for the Sunday. Excited, I put my name down. Sunday came but the game didn't. They had none left. Spoke to the lovely people at the Spirit Games stall and they said they had one at their shop. Tuesday came (closed Mondays!) and I piled the family into the car and said, right, we're going to Burton on Trent. So off we went and before I knew it, my daughter was clutching the box for me and my mission was accomplished. Played once so far, rulebook a bit tricky to follow but once figured out, it's a surprisingly easy but fun game.

RATING: 7.5

This time based action is very cool, but can lead to some slow points for players that invest a large amount of time to complete a specific action (such as a long excavation). This game is a very good example of integrating mechanics with theme.  Production quality on this game is top notch with one exception - the dial wheels (which are kinda cool) are slightly off-center making them harder to read than ideal.  Overall, the game provides a rich theme packaged in a clean well developed game.  Sure to please a wide spectrum of gamers.

RATING: 7.9
As has been pointed out by many others.  A strong Euro bolstered by a theme that compliments the mechanics quite well.  Unlike other games in it's class there is quite a bit of luck involved, but not so much as to cheapen the experience.  

In this game players are archaeologist questing for prestige and fame.  Each "dig" the players go on involve actually digging through a separate bag of chits in order to find precious valuables.  Of course the bag is filled with useless junk as well, and each trip to the dig site will be more of a gamble (as the ratio of junk to treasure gets worse and worse).

There's also a pleasing way to resolve who's turn it is.  Players manage how much time they spend doing things.  Players who are particularly clever (or lucky) will often be able to make as extra move or two before their turn is done.

Beyond those nifty lil aspects, the game is a pretty recognizable set-collection type.  Reminds me a lot of Ticket to Ride and Tikal.

RATING: 7
Nagyon kellemes játék, meg van benne a szokásos gyűjtögetős dolog az ebből adódó dilemmával: 4 féle módon szerezhetünk pontot, de a témaválasztás és a kivitelezés miatt jobb, mint az átlag. Jók az extra kártyák (autó, léghajó, ásó,...), és az idő múlása is jól ki van találva. És végre egy játék, amiben nincs pénz:)
Nekem úgy harangozták be, hogy a szerencse szerepe túl nagy. Nos ez teljesen igaz, az utolsó 15 darabból 1 db volt csak kincs, fölényesen ki is kaptam:) Ezzel együtt szívesen játszom még vele.
---
A magyar szabályban hiba van szerintem, mert azt írja, hogy 3 főnél a 16-os mezőről indulunk, de azt is írja később, hogy csak 2 és fél év ilyenkor a játék. Vagyis szerintem nem 16, hanem 26 a helyes!!!
Más: rámentem 3 szín gyűjtésére, nem számoltam azzal, hogy nagyon nem mindegy, melyik 2 szín marad ki. Fura volt azzal szembesülni, hogy nem tudok kiállítást szervezni, a végén megnéztem, 10-ből csak 1-et tudtam volna megszervezni. Utána végignéztem módszeresen a színeket, az eredmény: a 10 lehetséges színpárból 5 olyan van, aminek a hiánya 7 kiállítást üt ki, és 5 olyan, amelynek a hiánya 9-et. És egyébként nincs aszimmetria a dologban, bármelyik színhez található két másik, amelyek bármelyikével párt alkotva szívást jelentenek. Tehát ha valaki nem akar szívni, akkor vagy legalább 4 színből kell gyűjtenie, vagy utána kell nézni, hogy milyen színekre menjen. Segítek: ezeket a színpárokat nem szabad kihagyni: sárga+lila, sárga+kék, narancs+kék, narancs+zöld, lila+zöld. Családi játék ide vagy oda, szerintem ez nagyon gáz. Ha totál ugyanazt csinálom, csak épp a nem megfelelő színekkel, teljesen mások a lehetőségeim, és ezt semmi sem indokolja, ha nem néz utána az ember (és miért nézne egy családi játékban, hacsak nem ilyen hülye mint én), akkor a vakszerencsére van bízva. Oké tudom, régészet, de tematikailag ez már nem a régészet része, hanem a kiállítás.

RATING: 7
[COLOR=#959595][SIZE=7]Spiel des Jahres Nominee 2007[/SIZE][/COLOR]
[COLOR=#959595][SIZE=7]Deutscher Spiele Preis 9th Place 2007[/SIZE][/COLOR]

[i]Posted: 1/18/08, Rated: 6[/i]
{2P: ???, 3P: ???, 4P: 6}

[i]Thebes.[/i]  What a paradox.  On the surface, [i]Thebes[/i] is wonderfully rich and lustrous.  Mining below this façade can leave you feeling frustrated, empty, and unfulfilled.  How does one rate such an experience?

The time-track method of determining player order is novel and effective.  Imagine a long scale of action points, and every action you take from moving on the board, to drawing a card, to commencing an excavation requires points.  The person who is "last" on the timeline (farthest from the end of the game) is the next player to take her turn -- even if she just completed her previous move.  I really enjoyed this aspect of the game, and I hope to see it revisited in the future.

The game components are lavishly produced, and support the archaeology theme very well.  Players move between "modern" cities for research and exhibitions, and ancient sites in order to perform excavations.  Conducting research is accomplished by collecting sets of cards, choosing from four face-up cards.  That mechanic is growing tired.  I understand that it helps to mitigate the risk in a game, but it has been done to death.  Plus, there's an element of risk in this game which can't be mitigated.

That element is the bags which are used for excavations.  There are five archaeological sites, represented by five locations on the board.  Each site corresponds to a bag, inside of which are treasure tokens and "dirt" tokens.  Collecting larger sets of cards in a particular color allows you to draw more counters from a bag when you perform an excavation.  Allocating more action points to the excavation can also increase this number.  Yet despite collecting a large body of knowledge and devoting a lot of time to the excavation, you can come up empty.  In one game, I drew 10 counters from an excavation, 9 of which were useless.  It was phenomenally bad luck to be sure, but it killed my enthusiasm for the game.  In that same game, an opponent dedicated fewer resources to her excavation, and went 4-for-4 in finding treasures.  Argh!

There are other ways to score points, and I'd like to try an "excavation-less" strategy to see if that is viable.  Still, there's no getting around the uncertainty of the counter-drawing mechanism.  The exhibition "system" further rewards those players who were lucky with their excavations, fostering a situation where the rich get richer.  This game was wonderfully strategic and light, earning a solid "8" right up to those darned excavation bags.  Now I find myself wondering when I'll feel up to facing the potential heartbreak of future excavations.

An above average game, which I will play if I'm in the right mood?  That's the definition of a "6" on [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/profile.php?user=Beowulf]my scale[/url].  I might be able to dig up another ratings point after more explorations.


[i]Updated: 1/21/08, Rated: 7[/i]
{2P: 7, 3P: 8, 4P: 6}

It turns out that [i]Thebes[/i] doesn't scale very well.  My initial rating was based on a handful of games with four players.  The shortened timeline as well as increased competition for treasures made the luck of the "dig draw" an overwhelming component.

The game shines with three players, balancing multiple dig opportunities with competition for cards.  Sure, lucky draws still make a difference, but one or two bad draws aren't condemning.  The time factor feels about right with three players.

The game feels a little long when played with only two players, but it is still a better experience than when played with four.

RATING: 8
Light, fun , engaging. Excellent mechanisms. Always Play With Mik Svellov's Variant (remove 1 dirt tile per dig). Good googly-moogly, the bits in this game are nice.

Just let me start by saying, that the luck of the game doesn't bother me.

The main reason I play games is to have fun, and part of that fun can be matching wits with other players, but a large part of it can also be matching wits with the game engine itself. This is why games like Shadows over Camelot and Lord of the Rings are fun - they setup difficult tasks that take planning, execution, timing, and luck to overcome. Jenseits is not a cooperative game, but like a cooperative game, much of the fun from the game comes from doing well in spite of the obstacles in front of you. In JvT, those obstacles most often take the form of some measure of bad luck.

I wouldn't want to play games like JvT all the time, as it can get rather frustrating fighting that uphill battle too often, but if I am in the right mood for it, a fun game like JvT is right up my alley.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Most players will walk away from Thebes either totally enamored with the theme and mechanics or completely put off by the degree of randomness involved in carrying out excavations for artifacts and their victory points.  Players must juggle making the most efficient use of their 2/3 years of time with collecting the best knowledge cards, carrying out excavations at the 5 different dig sites before other players, and hosting exhibitions of their discovered artifacts throughout Europe.  Handling your time conservatively will allow you to potentially take more turns than your opponents, but neglecting to dig early enough might just mean that all of the valuable artifacts are gone before you get there.  The random luck (good or bad) of excavating for artifacts and lack of direct player interaction is probably enough to disinterest most "hardcore gamers", but Thebes makes for a fairly decent light gaming experience.

RATING: 9
A great game where the theme really shines.  I enjoy the mixture of strategy with randomness.  Also it requires a careful management of risk-vs-reward.  The way that time serves as the "currency" of the game is something I've never seen before, and I really enjoy.  The theme is so tightly woven into the gameplay, and it really shines well here.  Interesting theme and great mechanics make for a stellar game.

One of my favorite things in strategy games is when they offer multiple paths to victory, and I really see that in this game.  I love that you can go after sure points or hope for the lucky points when you go after a dig.  But, no matter how you slice it, luck, is going to play a big role in who wins the game.  If you have poor draws you will struggle to be victorious, but it's still a very fun game and the luck seems to fit the theme well.

Thanks again to my 2007 BGG Secret Santa!

RATING: 6.5
A Treasure excavation game with the theme in the same vein of Pergamon.
The time cost payment seems similar to Olympos and Glenmore. Probably Olympos may have taken this idea from Thebes.


Set-Up:
(1) Each player takes a set of (a) excavation permission, (b) 1 archaeologist, (c) 1 time market, and and (d) 1 time wheel

Remove 10 exhibitions cards from the card deck.   Shuffle the remaining cards, draw 4 cards from this and place them face up in space provided by the board.
Divide the remaining cards into 3 equal face down stacks.  Shuffle 5 small exhibition cards into the second stack and 5 large exhibition into the third stack

This set up seems similar to some other games like Pandemic and even Thunderstone (if we wish to set up the traps and rewards).
Place the year marker on 1901  with 2 or 3 players but 1902 with 4 players.
Place Archaeologist on Warschau 
Objective of the game: Gain most victory points.  How?

Player order - the player who has the time marker is the last takes his turn.  But if stacked, the player whose time marker is on top take his turn
Movement of archaeologist and action execution use weeks of time.

Set-up only use the first stack and the second stack.  Put the first stack on top of the second stack.

4 action can be executed depending on location
(a) Take a research card
(b) Exchange 4 cards displayed
(c) Execute an excavation
(d) Execute an exhibition

When exhibit cards is drawn, place face up on the space for exhibition space on the board.  Draw another card.
If the card supply exhausted the first time, shuffle the discards together with the third stack.  Place combined stack as new card supply.

Execute an Excavation
Player has a valid excavation permission and has 1 point of SPECIALIZED knowledge - either from researcher card or found item
Special knowledge - technical books matching the excavation Area
Rumor and Legends from the people in the excavation area
Assistants
So there is special knowledge, rumors and legends, and assistance
General knowledge
Assistant cards can still be used as a single point of knowledge but will have to be discarded after use.

Cannot use more points of general knowledge than the number point of special area knowledge he has.

Move the time wheel and decide how many weeks he want to use to dig.  Weeks are represent by black numbers.  The red indicate the number of excavation tokens he can take out from the bag.
Shovel allows players to take additional tokens out from the bag.

Bonus for the first excavation in an area.  Players may want to see who does what and where they would most likely want to go to avoid competing too much.

What is the strategy of this game?

When a player crosses space 52 a new year begin.  The player turn all his excavation permission to the valid side.

Important scoring - congress cards and special knowledge (5 points)
Compare the scoring system with Dice Town.
Looking through the rules, this game does not seem complicated.

RATING: 8
Excellent archeology game with thematic mechanics. Time is your primary currency and you spend it drafting research cards (which improve your chances of drawing valuable artifacts from dig site bags), congress cards (which share a cumulative point system of their own), shovels and research assistants (which also influence your digs). Alternatively, you can spend more time at a dig site but there is no guarantee that all this time will pay off. You can draw 10 chits from a bag and 7 could be blank "sand". But thematically it works and even though it's disappointing when it happens to you, it's entertaining when it happens to your opponent. Once you have enough artifacts you can hold an exhibit for additional points. Highly recommend this game if you can find a copy. All cards sleeved in excellent condition.

RATING: 8
Thebes is a very well themed game of Archaeology that combines a relatively unique time mechanism with the excitement of digging for treasure.

What I Like:
 - It takes a little while to explain the rules, but it is very accessible once you start playing.  Beginners will usually quickly pick up on the tactics they need to play well, and will be able to play competitively on the first game.
 - The time mechanism is great, and keeps everyone involved.  It is a little mini-game within the game to jockey for position on the time track so that you get your turns when you want them.  
 - Digging for treasure is very well implemented.  It's just plain fun to reach into a bag and pull out tokens, hoping to get treasure.  Since you throw the sand tokens you draw back into the bag and keep the treasure, the treasure sites gradually deplete.  This puts tension into the game, since you want to dig before everyone else, but you also want to collect enough knowledge first to make your digging effective.
 - The luck of the draw in digging vs. the control offered by collecting more cards is implemented very well.  The number of tokens you pull is influenced by the amount of time you spend to dig, and the amount of "knowledge" you have collected (you get this from cards).  So the game continually forces you to evaluate whether you have enough knowledge to make it worth it to go dig or not, and whether it's better to spend time on taking cards, or on extra digging.  This is where much of the game really happens, and it's a compelling decision.
 - The game plays quickly, and doesn't go too long - good length for the weight of the game.

What I don't like:
 - There is often a clearly optimal choice in the cards available, so not much thought required there.
 - It is quite possible to have no good cards available, no sites worth digging, and not enough time to both refresh the cards, and still have the next shot at them.  This is a frustrating situation.  
 - Continuing my gripes about the cards is that they are not all equally powerful.  In fact, there are some that are significantly better than others.  While I think the luck in digging is very well controlled by the game mechanics, the luck of the cards can easily favor one player heavily, if several good cards flip up at that player's location, on their turn.  This is the largest source of luck in the game, and is unsatisfying.  

Overall, this is a game that I am very glad to own.  I have introduced it to many non-gamers, always with very good results.  Personally, I doubt I will ever request it for myself, but I enjoy playing it, and there are many situations where it fits very well.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
They designed this just for me.  Between the archaeology theme and the timeline mechanic, I was hooked on this game even before I played it.

This is one example of where theme makes all the difference.  If the theme were removed, I'd find this interesting (the time mechanic), but frustrating (the chit pulls.)  WITH the theme, everything makes sense, and I find myself immersed in the game.  It just feels right -- the pressure to get there before the other guy balanced with the need for research; the frustration of an organized, well equipped dig coming up with almost nothing, while some tyro without a clue stumbles over a valuable artifact; the resentment when you're stuck in Greece on a long dig while the guys back home are getting fame and fortune on the conference circuit.  Fun game, for those who can exercise their imaginations.

RATING: 7
The Queen version of this game has the advantage that it basically works, while the original did not. The endgame freeze and the lame exhibitions are gone, along with some of the complicated end-game scoring - diversity is rewarded more subtly and cleanly now through a better exhibition mechanic.

So, the rather creaky original is now much cleaner and basically works. Is it any good? I'm not totally convinced. It's still hugely random, which may bug many (it doesn't bother me so much, and the game is at least fairly short). I think it has a better feel with 3 players than with 4 - the 4-player game feels a little truncated. The exhibitions are still a little dicey - they're all pretty hard to do unless you've already gotten lucky on your digs, especially the late-game exhibitions, and they don't offer that many points on the grand scale of things, especially when compared to their difficulty. The point rewards Conferences and high-value artifacts, both of which boil down basically to luck, are definitely somewhat out of line.

But, the game does basically work, the process of playing is fun, and it is unique and clever. The theme is rather well done (although as always, theme can be somewhat spoiled by weakness in the underlying game, which is the case here to some degree). I like it, and look forward to playing it a handful of times. Although it's clearly not a classic and won't have a ton of replay value, I expect it will remain on my shelf for a while on the combined merits of giving my game collection diversity points and having a nice theme.

RATING: 4
Updated 7/12/10.

I just don't understand the appeal of games this light.  When playing [thing=30869]Thebes[/thing], I feel a bit like I'm back playing Candyland or Snakes and Ladders with my family as a child.  That's not so say it's a bad game--it's quite good at what it does--but my enjoyment of board games is usually about grappling with the challenge, thinking, re-thinking, learning from my mistakes, and reveling in a victory that feels well earned.  On that scale, Thebes is roughly the equivalent of watching television.  Colorful, entertaining, but ultimately vapid.  

The mechanics of Thebes are very simple: move your researcher (or stay where you are) and perform one of four actions: take a card, take a different kind of card, replace the available cards, or dig for treasure.  If there's much difficulty to be found, it is in squeezing every last drop of efficiency from your movements around the board.  (As if we didn't have enough games in [i]that[/i] genre.)  Otherwise, it's move, draw, and hope.  That's the game.  In light of that, the 60-75 minute play time seems far too long.   

I can see where Thebes would fit with some people's groups (and I think I can find a place with it with my group with four players), but it just seems sort of pointless.

RATING: 6
Rated 8 after a first play with four players. OK, yes, this is a very random game. But this is actually one of the better "push your luck" games I've played. There's enough going on beyond the simple token pulling from the bag to keep things interesting. 

The time track for turn order is very clever. The movement on the board tests a player's efficiency in maximizing actions. And, I'll be darned, it's just fun pulling tokens from the bag and dealing with the luck of the draw. If it was a simple deck of cards, it would not have the same effect.

This is solid family gaming. I'm not sure if this holds up over multiple plays, but I'm really hoping that this works as a two player game. We shall see.

Update after a couple of two player games--solid game. It's not incredibly tense, and it's not among the best two player games I own, but a two player game of Thebes is relaxing and fun. This is a great game for an occasional option for something different.

Update: Looking at my earlier comments, I can see that I was unwittingly foreshadowing the "been there, done that" aspect of the game. Five plays was probably enough, and neither my wife or I felt inclined to reach for this one any more. Traded this away for Dominion: Dark Ages.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
We play 2-player, which takes about an hour and fifteen minutes, but feels a little shorter than that.  There is most certainly luck of the draw (both in drawing from the bags and in card flips), but if you are patient, the luck seems to largely even out over the course of the game.  The draw bags hold both archeological treasures and blank "dirt" tiles.  Any dirt tiles pulled are put back in the bag afterwards, so the more treasures that have been taken from an area, the higher the odds of getting dirt next time.  During the game, players become more powerful in certain colors, granting them more pulls when they're there, but with lower odds of getting good stuff.  So if someone gets exceptionally good pulls once, then they probably won't be too lucky next time.  So far, the exhibition cards haven't been a big factor in our games, as they seem to leave the game as quickly as they come in, before anyone has time to claim them.  You have to manage your position on the time track carefully, as whoever is farthest behind always goes next, even if it means one person takes several turns before their opponent goes again.  If you are drawing for treasures, it may be to your advantage to spend less time there (taking fewer pulls from the draw bag) if it allows you to go again, even though it decreases your chance of pulling treasure.  Overall, the game is excellent and works very nicely with the theme.  Also, the pieces and the artwork of the game are really nice, especially the draw bags.

Social.
House rule from user 'fluxx' After digging for artefacts you have to spend one week for each artefact you found for analyzing and catalogizing... (This is to cut down the luck-factor a bit without changing the core-mechanic.) In exchange the game lasts half a year longer.

What happens at the end of the game, when pulling extra artifacts would take you past the end of the year?

you have to time your last digging properly - usually you try to have 1-3 weeks left for the case you are lucky. If you left 2 weeks and find 3 artefacts you have to chose two of them - the third one doesn't count (never happened yet)

RATING: 8
I believe this was the game that started off BGG trading for me. Thanks Thebes!

This theme is so neat. There is a great luck factor in this game, but it's thematic and I love it. There is a great chance you will prepare for a dig and come up with nothing. Such is life. We play that when making a pull, if you pull any dirt out of the bag, you put all the dirt back in except (1). Thematically this makes sense to us that if an area of ground is disturbed, looking for treasure, there will be some soil that won't make it back in the hole. The way the turns work by utilizing a catchup mechanic for those who take less time to perform actions is really neat. Another unique one to have in the collection. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Love it
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Just like 'Around the World in 80 Days', this is a good, well-themed medium-light family strategy game, where Lady Luck  occupies a crucial role. And, do you know what? I don't (really) care because theme and mechanics mesh so extremely well and everybody is the archaeologist of their own fortune in this game (well, maybe not always).

The player order mechanic is original (at least, I haven't seen it before) and works very well. Artwork and components are absolutely gorgeous (I like those chrono-whatsits).

All in all, a game whose hugely luck-driven mechanics are cleverly justified by its theme, which as an extra benefit makes it easy to pick up and is still quite a lot of fun for hardcore gamers. 

It does take too long though, considering its huge luck factor.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
What a well-themed game!

The thing that I like best about Thebes is the time/turn mechanic. You choose actions that take variable amounts of time, advance on the calendar...and then move again when anyone behind you on the calendar has already moved. Its possible to do 2 or more moves in a row, under the right conditions.

The excavation bags are fun, too. The whole theme seems really well integrated into the gameplay.

Its important to recognize that the game has a very large luck component. I can forgive that in a game that plays quickly and lightly, as Thebes does. But if the game is taken too seriously or begins to drag, the luck detracts from the enjoyment for me.

I'd play this any time and may consider trading for a copy some time. I'd really like to see the turn/time mechanic appear in a heavier, less-lucky game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Thebes is a reimplementation of Jenseits von Theben, a charming lighter game that originally used card decks in place of the chit bags to simulate archaeological digs. I honestly don't see much difference between the two editions, this one looks nicer and is easier to get, so it would be the one I recommend for most.

In short, players have the choice of building up their knowledge base in Europe by acquiring books, shovels, and research assistants, or traveling to archaeological sites and digging away. The more knowledge you have, the easier it is to pull more chits out of the bag which makes it more likely that you will gain some of the VPs that are hidden amongst the dirt.

The chief scoring methods are either getting a lot of artifacts of high value, or or showing off a certain collection of artifacts (i.e.: 2 greens and a yellow, regardless of value) for a quick bonus.

This game was also notable for being one of the first to introduce the "time based action point" system where player actions have a time cost that is marked on the board. The person who has used the least time / APs during the game gets to take the next turn, so you can spend a lot of points on one big dig, but then have to wait a long time for your next turn (which can cost you: i.e.: if an exhibition comes up and you can't get there due to not having a turn come around for so long). This was also used in Neuland, and both of the original games, I think, were released in the same year. I find it delightful, and prefer it here of those 2.

A lot of the game is luck driven. The deck that dictates what type of knowledge is available is one of those, the other, more obvious one being the actual chit draw from the bag. Sure, you can load up on knowledge and have a virgin bag, but still end up drawing garbage. The game is maybe 33% too long for that to be totally acceptable, but I'm still largely OK with it because it fits the theme so well.

If you are willing to put up with some swings based on a tile draw, then I think this is a great gateway game. I am certainly in the mood for it on occasion, I just wish it scaled to 5 instead of the 4 limit.

RATING: 7
A good Euro that meets most of the criteria for a better game of the genre.  I don't think THEBES is _nearly_ as thematic as many have made it out to be though- it seems like theme-deprived Eurogamers consider anything with something like the week-based timer or the archaeological digs in the game to be "rich with theme".  It's not any more thematic than most of the better Euros.  It's a fun, fairly light game that plays very much like those pre-SETTLERS Euros put out by Ravensburger, Jumbo, Piatnik, et. all.  I really like the digging part of the game although it is pretty much like a lottery- but it's fun to go try to dig up the last handful of treasures and then run off to Moscow to give a lecture before going to Paris to supply artifacts for an exhibition.  There's plenty of luck, some drama, and a pretty decent narrative overall. The components are very nice but for a retail price $15-$20 I could have done without the custom printed bags and eight rulebooks in various langauges.  I do think the game is a little too long for what it is (if it were 60-75 minutes it'd be just right) and I'm not a big fan of any kind of card drafting mechanic but for a light, accessible game with a lot of appeal it succeeds.

RATING: 5
Update: Shine coming off this one. The combination of only a few digs per player (each with a huge random element) combined with extremely tactical play limit it to being very fun pretty much only with kids.
-----
Was a hit with the fam. There are just enough research "options" for me to make choosing the development path interesting, without it becoming a card-driven game. There's also just enough luck that my whole family (even my competitive son) will fun no matter who wins. Turns are really fast, at least until the last year, when people start analyzing odds at each remaining excavation site, based on the artifacts recovered so far. A very interesting twist is the idea of the garbage tokens which get thrown back into the bag. Which means that the odds of finding something good at each sight [i]tend[/i] to go down incrementally for every token excavated.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
A conceptually simple game built around a nice timing mechanism reminiscent of titles like Olympos and Tinners' Trail hampered by two major game-technical errors: the vast luck of the draw in excavating, and the pointlessness of exhibitions which come and go at unpredictable moments. The luck of the draw while excavating is truly inexcusable: it may be 'thematically correct' in that you never know what you dig up, but it is certainly no fun whatsoever if a major dig you've been gearing up for turns up absolutely nothing. The game offers zero compensation for such occasions. Then the exhibitions: these take some planning to succeed with, but because their arrival and departure is also uncertain it is simply too much effort to consciously go for these. You should treat them as useful bonuses if and only if they happen to be easily obtainable.

In other words: seriously overrated title, to be avoided, even when primarily played in a family environment. There are other titles (also from Queen, if you value such details) which function better as a [i]game[/i].

RATING: 7

Eventually you go dig for artifacts and you can increase your odds with good play, it is still random. Once you have artifacts you can display them for bonus points. You are also able to try and go before congress to get points. You want to avoid specializing in one area as it will deplete and your bonus will cap out.

RATING: 6
Components: All-around great quality on the artwork and iconography. The board, cards, and artifact tokens are linen-coated. I'm especially impressed by the five color-coded draw bags, where the publisher could have gotten away with just having decks of cards that we needed to constantly shuffle.

Experience: Lots of fun. It's very tense, using up my limited days to build up knowledge, then racing to the dig sites. It's satisfying to dig for artifacts; the draw-bag mechanism works well to convey the theme. It's tough to know how much time to spend in both places, as it can be almost as valuable to just hang out in Europe as to go digging for treasure.

[Update]I've soured on this title. Too much luck; not enough thrill from the digging.

RATING: 10
My comments from the original version are shown below, but this new version adds improved art and production, simpler exhibitions, a wheel replaces the dig chart, and players pull artifact tokens from separate site dig bags. All improvements, for the most part. This game is now getting the recognition it deserves, including a Golden Geek Award for Best Family Game of 2008.

Desktop-Published edition:
"Wow, what a brilliant representation of a theme. Players are archaeologists competing for artifacts buried under five different dig sites. At the end of the game, players earn victory points for the artifacts they have found, the knowledge they have gained, and the exhibitions they have won, which then determines the winner of the game. Each turn the player can take one of four actions: move to another location, choose a card that corresponds to the current location, start an excavation, or pass. Much like Neuland, each action will take a specific number of weeks and the player furthest back in the timeline is always the first in turn order. This presents some interesting situations, because when one person goes for a high value card or a lengthy dig, they will move to the front of the queue, which gives other players potentially multiple actions before catching up and then passing the leader. Players collect cards at various European cities to gain knowledge of each dig site and to provide various special actions. If the player has the proper knowledge cards, an excavation can be made. During an excavation, the player cross-references the number of corresponding knowledge cards they have to the number of weeks they want to spend in order to determine the number of cards to pull from a random assortment. Cards will either provide Artifact VP cards or garbage that gets shuffled back into the deck, which means that each successive dig has a higher chance of pulling up garbage. Whenever an Exhibition card is drawn, it is placed on the timeline by the specified number of weeks ahead of the trailing player. Any player at the proper location at the specified time can exhibit their Artifacts. Players add each of a particular type or types of Artifacts with the roll of a die. The player with the highest total gets the Exhibition card, which is worth victory points at the end of the game. As I stated earlier, I think the game mechanics support the theme quite well; the timeline usage, the diminishing returns for excavations, and the exhibition of artifacts. Once you manage to decipher the rules, the game itself clips along at a good pace. The artwork and production in the Prinz Spiele version of the game that I have leave a little to be desired, but the new version looks to be an improvement. The game is innovative, well thought out, and it plays quickly. This is a really solid design and I highly recommend it. It is one of my top ten favorite games of 2004."

RATING: 8
Players spend time (weeks, basically money) acquiring knowledge and equipment to outfit their archaeological expedition to up to five ancient sites.  The sites are represented by five decks of "treasure" cards, that are also filled with a lot of dirt!  Time spent on site allows cards to be flipped and hopefully treasure found.  Points at the end of the game come from treasure, winning exhibitions, attending congresses (collecting a type of card during the game), and knowledge gained.  Belongs in the same category of "experience" games such as Safe Return Doubtful and Legend of the Lost Dutchman.  Very good, and really enjoyable game.  Played with "clear the board", "lots of treasure", and "hard excavation table" variants.

Queen Edition: stunning, lovely job.  Cleaned up some of the slightly messy bits - exhibitions are cleaner (no weeks offset or battle, just a sliding track and first there claims it); single assistants and shovels now do something; bonus for first dig at each site, etc.  Great game.

RATING: N/A
● The best part of the game that I like was the digging part. You get to excavate for artifacts, and for this, what you'll do will be resembled by drawing out tokens from the cloth bag. There are artifacts and dirt tokens in the bag. You need to know how weeks you're willing to spend on the excavation spot. The longer you're staying, the better chance you get in your findings. To do this, you need to prepare yourself before the excavation by going to parts of Europe to study or to do some research about your location of diggings.
● These will be represented by the coloured books on the cards that you obtained during your stay at parts of the Europe. Although it seems like easy for you to take, going to places to get the cards will also required you to spend weeks for the whole journey.
● How does the week play any role you say. Well, in our 3 players game, game will end when the time track reaches 2 and a half year, that's 130 weeks. So every single thing that you do will require you to sacrifice some time (in this case weeks), digging, researching, etc. So you need to manage everything in under 130 weeks to gain the most VPs.

Summary: Not buying as I don't feel anything special about the game. This is probably because some of the mechanics from the game have few similarities from other games that I own.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After first play:

An interesting enough game to warrant further plays. The time track that works as a turn order is my favorite mechanic of the game. This creates a good amount of tension when deciding where to go, what to take and when to go digging.

Speaking of digging...I like the dials that indicate how long/how much you can pull from the bag. And pulling from the bag created some fun moments of everyone hoping the digger pulls 5 worthless tokens...Then seeing it happen!

Of the Queen games I've played lately (Alhambra, Escape, Treasure Hunter, Kingdom Builder and this one) Thebes is hands down the better of the five in my opinion.

Pros
+ Strategic and fun gameplay
+ Components are good
+ Playtime and pace is good (80 min for 4p game)
+ Enough there to satisfy a more seasoned player, yet simple enough for younger players

Cons
- a good Queen game, but still a Queen game and for me personally they've all felt just a little lacking so far.

Good game for families, would play again.

Update: Bought for $20, worth it!

RATING: 5
Not sure what people are smoking when they play this game, but I'd really like you to share it with me.

Thebes is a game I wanted to like.  It's got standard Queen production values (a good thing) and is actually quite thematic in the way its moving parts click together.  However, while I am not adverse to luck, this game is entirely a crapshoot when it comes to any strategy you might formulate.

In short, you gather knowledge in different colors and each bit of knowledge allows you to pull out more tiles from a bag of the corresponding dig site when you're excavating.  The trick of it is that there is treasure in each dig site, but there's also worthless dirt to be found.

Now here's the thing - you can load up on all the knowledge you want and all the possible bonus combos you can in order to increase your odds, sort of like a CRT in a wargame - CRTs generally can be manipulated so that even on your worst roll, you'll still come out on top or at least break even.  Only here, you can gather up everything to increase your odds to the very best possible chance of getting good stuff, but the sheer amount of dirt tiles in every site can destroy your investment where you put yourself at a massive disadvantage by shortening the amount of turns you'll get, walking away with absolutely nothing.  Among 4 players, this occurred SEVERAL times.  I'd be okay with the random poor draw, but for this to happen over and over again seemed wrong.

In this manner, you're better of simply flipping a coin and betting on the exact number of heads or tails - you have about the same random chance of winning that game as you do at winning Thebes and that does not make a very good game.

I'm not averse to luck-based games, mind you.  I quite particularly love randomness and chance, but asking me to invest 2 hours in a game where a good portion of it is strategy, but the end result is completely and totally out of your control?  No.  No, thank you.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Thebes is a fairly light and highly thematic game where players are archaeologists traveling around Europe gathering knowledge, assistants, and equipment.  The turn order is based on who is farthest behind on the time track.

Once you feel you have enough to set out on a dig, you may travel to 5 different sites and decide how much time you want to spend digging.  The amount of knowledge you have garnered and the amount of time you spend will determine how many tokens you can draw from the dig site's draw bag.  Artifact tokens score their printed point values and may also be used later to score in exhibitions.  Of course, you also have a good chance of drawing blank "dirt" tokens.

Drawing from a bag that is half full of "dirt" is quite luck dependent, but players can mitigate this by gathering more knowledge, assistants, and shovels to maximize their draws.  The luck of the bag draw is acceptable to me because it fits the theme well.  Do you race off to dig first with minimal requirements and low chances of finding anything, or do you spend extra time to increase your odds but risk someone else looting all the good stuff first?  The choice is yours!  

Unless you are completely adverse to luck, I would highly recommend this game! 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
(6/13) Only one play. Strong theme.  Fun to play but very chance-driven and random.  Someone else on BGG said "Let's just roll a D4 to see who wins.  Oh, you'd rather play it out? Okay." That's a bit harsh but I understand the sentiment.  Very interesting mechanics in this game; it uses a time track like Red November.  That's clever and adds a layer of strategy, but it still kind of boils down to who is more "skilled" at blindly pulling stuff out of a bag. * Edit: Rating dips from 8 to 6. I love the theme, components, and mechanics. But. There is a lot of luck in this game. A lot. I'm tempted to say it's almost entirely luck. There are not multiple paths to victory in this game. It all comes down to luck on the digs. Collecting lecture cards and end game bonuses for having the most specific knowledge are no match for artifact points. It's a very fun ride with the right group, but this is definitely a game that plays YOU, not the other way around.

RATING: 7
Juego aceptable y original. Cada jugador representa un arqueólogo que debe ir ganando puntos a base de ir consiguiendo conocimientos en diferentes ciudades europeas para luego intentar hacer excavaciones provechosas en  paises/regiones de gran tradición arqueológica. Es original en los tiempos de juego, pues siempre juega el más atrasado y vamos empleando semanas para adquirir conocimientos o mejorar nuestras escavaciones o conseguir contactos en las zonas a escavar; también es original en el modo de realizar las escavaciones, pues rebuscamos "x" reliquias dentro de la bolsa de la región escavada(eso remarca el caracter de búsqueda) en tal o cual región empleando más o menos semanas. Este segundo punto tiene un inconveniente y es que lo hace muy aleatorio el juego, pues podemos encontrar sólo escombros durante muchas semanas de búsqueda y uno que emplee poco tiempo y tenga más suerte puede coger reliquias de mucho valor; pero yo creo que eso representa en sí­ mismo la "vida del arqueólogo"
Para ganar: son muy importantes los medios de transportes(Un coche al principio de la partida te ahorrará unas 10 semanas en toda la partida que podrás emplear en otros menesteres); los conocimientos generales son buenos pero amí me gustan más los "ayudantes" que te dan conocimientos específicos de todas las culturas; a veces es mejor no moverte y coger lo cercano que irte muy lejos porque perderás muchas semanas (p.ej: sólo debemos ir a Moscú cuando haya al menos 2 cosas porque está muy lejos). Escavar primero te da más opciones de tener éxito. Acude, siempre que puedas, a escavar el territorio menos esquilmado o el que te permita hacer exposiciones.Importante las bonificaciones finales de 5 puntos para el que más sepa específicamente de cada civilización
PARTIDAS
25-04-2008 Partida de estreno; 4 jugadores (Tyrion, Juas, Pi y Gato, o sea, yo). Empieza, por azar, Pi que tiene la suerte de pillar un coche que le ahorra movimientos durante toda la partida; Juas y Laura se dedican a coger conocimientos especí­ficos, mientras que yo tengo la suerte de poder coger varios ayudantes; de las 5 primeras excavaciones Tyrion hace 2 y los demás una. Yo estoy  "concentrado" y hago 2 prospecciones increíbles dejando muy esquilmadas Palestina (sobretodo) y Egipto; Pi empieza a recolectar conocimientos genéricos en cada excavación a donde va; Pi y yo cogemos unos congresitos ; Juas hace 1 exposición, Tyrion otra y yo 2; al final la partida termina con VICTORIA del Gato (es decir, yo) con 54 puntacos; Juas segundo con 47, Tyrion con 36 es tercera y Pi que por sus infructuosas escavaciones sólo consigue 17 puntitos. Se cumple la cosumbre que es que Juego que estrenamos entre nosotros 4, juego que les gano ;-)

-------------Original rating 24/2/16 Score:6
[b]Rating based on - 5 plays, 2 player[/b]
Got this in a Math Trade a few months ago and was super enthusiastic to play. It wasn't complete so when I finally got to play it was very exciting because I'd looked forward to it for a long time. It's a fun game with some great components - the Indiana Jones figures and the cool time wheels especially. It definitely is a light game with a lot of luck and not a lot of decisions to make, but I like it much better than any of the other Queen games I've tried so far. However, I also got a copy of Thebes:The Tomb Raiders in a subsequent Maths Trade and we like that one just a shade better than this. The other half thinks that Thebes is too repetitive - move, collect books, move collect books, for a year or a year and a half, then move, dig, move, dig for the rest of the game. I don't mind that too much because we do a fair bit of collecting in Ticket to Ride, but with so many other really good game options, this doesn't look likely to get played too often. It also doesn't have anything particularly unique to make me keep it in the collection.
[b]Status: TRADED for Castle Panic[/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
The whole mechanic of digging in a bag for treasure is  very thematic and fun.  There are definitely meaningful decisions (do I play conservatively for points I know I'll get, or take the risk of digging in the hope of big points?), and although there is a luck element, the fun factor is very high! 

In one respect it could be argued that this game has a limited appeal - the rule complexity is too high for non gamers, whereas the luck element is too high for gamers.  It would be a great gateway game if the rules were less complex; a great gamers game if there was less luck - so it doesn't really satisfy either group.  It has limited replay value for gamers; non-gamers who make it through the rules probably will have a lot of fun with it however.

But for with the same group, it is a great family game: kids love the luck element, and once they have the rules down, you can finish a game in under an hour.  I've revised my initial skepticism - I don't think it will see much play as a serious competitive game for adults, because of the luck element, but as a family game for kids, it's great.

See my review and interview with my 8 year old and 10 year old about the game here:
http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/350381

RATING: 5
This one was fun for a few plays early on in my re-entry into the hobby. The components are spectacular,  the time mechanism for actions is brilliant, and the theme shines through. It seems like a great game. Unfortunately, it doesn't hold up as anything more than a family game due to the randomness throughout the design. I've still yet to see the exhibits used much at all due to the way they come and go. I think a tweak is necessary to get these guys in the game. Fortunately, Martin Wallace used the time mechanism in Tinners Trail so that juicy bit is not wasted. 

After our last game, a friend suggested a strategy that seemed compelling: you stay in Europe the whole game and never go digging. You simply collect congress cards and load up in expertise in the various categories for endgame points. As these are sure things vs digging, you would rack up a lot of certain points. Never tried it, but sure sounds like the way to go, albeit quite unthematic.

RATING: 7.5
Great theme, great fun. Highly recommended as a gateway. 

Even playing it as a newbie, it reminded me very much of Ticket to Ride + Alhambra. Set collection and maximizing time+location is the central theme to this game. 

The luck element - drawing in archaeological digs kept it light and fun. Every dig was a group event we kept highly interactive (house rule we learned from a friend: have other people do your digs for you - creates an interesting dynamic of blame/gratitude). 

Ultimately, though, it's the luck that turns both me and fiance off to this game. It's not just in the digs, it's in the cards that show up. I think I prefer it to TTR, but will only play with gateway gamers when heavy games are not an option

Component Quality: :star::star::star::star::star:
Player Interaction: :star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
"Take That" Conflict: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Theme Integration: :star::star::star::star::star:

One great thing about this game is that half of the rules can be explained after the game starts as different kind of researcher and exhibition cards come up.  I may need to bump up my "strategic depth" rating, because there is actually a fair bit of planning to do if one is to maximize their potential for doing as many digs as possible each year in the most efficient way.  After reading many comments on this game, I thought it might be a total luckfest, with few meaningful decisions, but I don't have these sentiments myself.  When to dig in a given location can be a very difficult decision for which several factors should be brought into consideration.

A game this high in luck will never get higher than about a 9 for me, but this game is awfully fun, and gets very high marks regardless.

RATING: 7.5
The strategy in this game is a bit light for my tastes, but the digging mechanic is very interesting. Many say it's too luck based, but that's the only redeeming quality for me. This game is about doing your best to control that randomness by doing longer expeditions with more information, so as to draw as many tokens as possible. 

While most of the mechanics are straightforward and even clever, it is very obtuse in a few areas. One example is the automobile versus the blimp. The Automobile card lets you travel faster and is always active. Meanwhile the Blimp is a 1-time use card. That all seems reasonable except that there is no indication of any of that on the cards themselves. I only found those rules in a little example box in the rulebook.

Ultimately a very interesting game that I think kids may enjoy as well.

RATING: 7
Archeology-themed board game.  People on BGG like to bitch about the "luck factor" of digging for artifacts by drawing chits blindly from the dig bags.  Ignore these boring people.  That's one of the best parts of the game.  It's like pulling a slot machine.  It keeps people not in the immediate hunt for the lead interested as they always have a gambler's chance to get back in the game.  If you turn your nose up at this game, you need to loosen up.

Update: Having played this a few times now, I can see why people gripe about the luck, especially people who prefer the deeper games.  You just have to take this game for what it is.  You can play optimally on the board and still lose with bad pulls.  Knowing that may happen can dampen enthusiasm to replay a bit.  I'd still play it, though.  It's fun.

RATING: 5
I have wanted to play this game for quite a long while. I expected everything given the complaints, namely that the luck of the draw overwhelms any sense of strategy or fairness. That being said, I think the theme is glorious and I was ready to win or lose based on the chit pull, knowing that some better pulls could lose to weaker ones. All part of the theme! Overall, I found the set collection of the game felt quite dated and uninteresting. Although the time track was probably the most interesting part of the game, I often wanted to play less optimally only so that I didn't have to wait an exceedingly long time for my turn to come up again. The option to playing more efficiently was there, but at the cost of getting cards that you want. Again, that's fine but it made the decisions rather limited. When it came to the chit pull, I was fine with the random luck - but there were so many blank tiles that it made the experience more frustrating than fun. Perhaps a variant could be introduced where pulling a certain number of sand chits reduces the time? Anyway, overall Thebes was a rather boring experience of a game. A real shame considering the great theme and quality components.

RATING: 9
Believe the hype. This one is fun, with a capital 'F'. So few eurogames have a Theme that fits well with how the game plays, and this is one of those treasures. Rummaging around in the bags, really gives one the feel of 'digging' for artifacts, and travelling Europe in search of information and research to help discover more artifacts when you dig really sells the theme of being an archeologist.

It's more of a gateway game, though, and not one I would place in front of anyone who's enamoured with Power Grid, or Caylus, as the strategy to this game is managing, and minimizing your luck. Although, in all the games we've played so far, the scores are VERY close, even though many seem to point out that this game will suffer from the 'Runaway Leader' syndrome. I haven't found this to be the case at all, and it should be a great hit with casual gamers and families alike.

The kids love this. Just for digging in the bags, if nothing else.

RATING: 7.25
I definitely want to play this again to see how it would play out a second time around. The logical pattern of the game is to collect knowledge, dig at sites, then go off to exhibit (collecting exhibit cards for victory points), but that's not necessarily the way things will work out. I found myself wanting for knowledge cards to dig at some sites because I threw all my archaeological eggs, as it were, into a single basket. I redeemed myself by collecting Congress cards for a nice infusion of points. The plus side to going after a lot of one set of knowledge cards for a site is that you are sure to receive the bonus for it at the end. The drawback is that you'll be lagging behind in terms of being able to dig successfully at other sites, which, in turn, feeds into your ability to nab the exhibition cards for additional victory points. You find yourself trying to balance the cost of your actions, the net gain of your actions and the future repercussions of your actions. It makes for some intriguing decision making and a thoroughly fun game. Interestingly, the player who collected the most exhibition cards in our game finished third in points. I would've thought he'd come in first. Ultimately, the player who dug the most won.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Daughter purchased with her allowance. Initial impression. Thebes really makes players feel as if they're in a race to grab the resources they need to loot the cultural heritage of other nations, abscond with their finds, and gain fame in the "civilized" world for doing so. While it may be historically accurate regarding the conduct of archeology in the period in which it is set, unfortunately this is not what I want my kids to think archeology should be. It also bothers me that "knowledge" is gained by reading books in Europe, not from the hard work of conducting a meticulous excavation. Furthermore, it seems unthematic that all debris is always returned to sites after digging. 

Mechanically, while there's a lot of luck in the game, it bothers me less than I feared. Having experienced the time track in Patchwork, however, I don't like that you can't look ahead beyond the four available cards to plan your moves—there's a designer's variant from the original JvT that we may try to address this. Exhibitions appear underpowered, and seem to be something you do when either it's convenient or you don't have something better to do (like dig), but perhaps this would change with more than 2p or more experience. 

At least as 2p, the arc of the game is anticlimactic. The first year has good excitement as you race to gather adequate resources to start digging when sites are fresh and hope for valuable find from smaller digs. The second year is more workman-like as you build up for and execute bigger digs that should yield good finds, but can only disappoint if luck is against you. The final year drags on as you try to eek out a few more points here and there. I'd say the game feels at least half a year and 20 minutes too long. 

I'm interested in trying some variants to address some of these issues, especially as my daughter seems to like the game quite a bit more than I do.

UPDATE: Played 3p for the first time. Positively, competition for cards meant that players were forced to adopt different strategies, so the game felt more varied than at 2p. Negativity, the game felt more random due to fewer opportunities for back to back turns. The game still felt too long.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.8
[gameid=30869] has an extremely well-integrated theme. I'm not sure how one could better capture in a board game what it's like to be an archeologist.

A lot of people complain about the game being too random, but really, your risks are usually manageable. I think the reason that Thebes appears much more random than most games is because its randomization mechanism is unfamiliar. It's easy to see the odds in rolling a pair of dice, but the probabilities involved in drawing tiles from a bag that is increasingly filled with blanks are less obvious. I have heard many people say that the game's victory comes down to pure, wild luck; in my experience, this is simply not the case.

RATING: 7
I am giving this one a conditional 7: on the condition that it is played with some sort of variant to how you dig (i.e., how you draw treasures from the bags). Currently, digging is way too random, and can undo any good strategy you might have had (or reward the player with the worst strategy).

With a digging variant in place, this is a solid game, with a neat theme and a good execution.

RATING: 4
Nice mechanic for turn order, using "time" as a resource. You move around spending weeks and collecting cards that will help you in your excavations, but these consist in drawing random tokens from several bags. The huge luck factor certainly fits the theme, but it still spoils the game for me. Too often you prepare yourself well for an excavation, dig a decent number of tokens and come out almost empty-handed. Then a player with less preparation and less time investment takes one or two tokens from the same bag and scores big. The problem is not only that half the tokens are worth nothing, it's also (and mainly) that the values of the artifacts vary wildly. Luck of the draw with the cards is quite significant, too. The game might be bearable for 30-45 minutes, but my experience has been more in the 90-120 minute range.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
this one just seemed to strike a chord with me.  it's a game of archeology.  each player visits cities to research dig sites.  then they visit dig site and try to excavate artifacts.  lastly, if possible, players can exhibit their artifacts from the digs.  there are several mechanics that I like, some driving up the luck factor but very fitting for the theme.  There is a time line that runs around the board.  Each activity takes time, and player's move their pawns along it based on the length of time certain activities require.  The next players turn is whoever is on the top of the stack on the day that is earliest on the time line.  And the game ends once everyone as made it a predetermined number of times around the time line (twice, or two years, for a four player game).  

Each dig site has a bag full of chits that you pull out of its bag.  Some of the chits are just dirt and some are artifacts.  At the end of your turn, you keep the artifacts you've discovered, and the dirt goes back in the bag, reducing the odds that more artifacts will be found.   

giving it a 9, though it be the something-new-and-shiny effect.

RATING: N/A
use Jenseits rules suggested in Comment comparing versions "There are several differences between the rules to Jenseits and Thebes, some of which can be incorporated into the newer version. For instance the original game gives a scoring bonus to the most diversified player, knowledge wise. Players compare their worst area of knowledge and the player who has the most knowledge in this worst area scores 7 points to the best of my recollection. (Knizia like scoring)
 In the original game 9 random cards (tokens) are removed from each dig site to create imperfect knowledge of what can be found and the dirt to treasure ratio is then also uncertain. I believe you could probably do this to the new version if you wanted.

 The way the exhibits work between the two games are totally different and cannot easily be integrated. Jenseits has cards which announce an exhibit, how far in the future it will be held, and what regions treasures are to be judged. On that exact date all players at the site of the exhibit would count their eligible treasures and roll a d6 to determine the value of their exhibit and the highest exhibit value would win the exhibit card. In Thebes you just show up with the required colored treasure and win the exhibit. The original version forces a lot more thought to win the exhibits so is probably a little less family oriented. 

I believe there is also a new type of card in the new version but this has little impact on game play. "

RATING: 7
Thebes is a very accessible game about archaeological digs around the turn of the century. They go out of their way to provide background and pictures of each artifact found, even though mechanically they just do things like "+5 Points". 

The game consists of moving around to various European cities and doing research, then finally journeying to places like Egypt and Mesopotamia to excavate with the knowledge you have obtained. Optimization of your limited actions leads you to want to do lots of research first, but the game also strongly incentivizes being the first player to perform a dig. This tension is at the heart of the game.

The complaint that people make over and over again is due to the randomness of the digs, which can result in large swings of fortune. A single tile can grant anywhere from 0 to 7 points, a significant number considering that our first game saw 3 players have final scores within a span of 4 points. However, there are other effective point-scoring strategies that are far less dependent on these random draws. So ultimately, a player will decide how much of his/her final score he/she wishes to leave to the whims of fate.

It might help to have the points for exhibitions scale with the number of players.  It's a very cool idea for a game, and I love a lot of the mechanisms, but it feels about 90% finished.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
My family and I found Thebes to be a very mediocre game.  I mainly purchased the game for my wife, as she enjoys playing games with my son and I, but she was showing signs of getting burned out on the heavier games we play.  From reading the reviews and ratings, it sounded like this game would be a nice change of pace for her.  And it turns out it is exactly that (thanks, fellow BGGers).

We found it to be a simple game (perhaps a little *too* simple for me), as it is very light and easy to play game.  And I have to agree with others that state that “digging for treasures” (blinding picking out pieces from a bag and hoping they are treasure pieces) is indeed fun and adds some suspense/drama.  And it certainly is easy to carry conversations with others (because of the simplicity) if you are into the more social aspects of gaming.  Also, there is a ton of luck involved.  That does not bother me (as I enjoy a mixture of game types), but I know others are more sensitive to this.  The positive is that anyone can pick this up and have a decent chance of winning (certainly a good chance of being competitive).

However, simplicity has its cost.  While I just mentioned a positive of the luck factor, the negative is that little analytical skill is needed for this one.  Sure, you’ll need to decide how best to use your time (“weeks”), but all in all, I found it not very stimulating on the thinking side.  Again, that is not necessarily bad for a change of pace, but not something I want to play often.  Another downside is, after a few games, I noticed that we would all pretty much play the same every time.  We would invest our time in picking up research items, and then someone would finally decide to rush down and do a dig, which would cause everyone else to hurry and do the same.  There’s really nothing necessarily wrong with this, as I think this is the nature of the game, but I found it to be a little bit monotonous in this regard.

Finally, I must admit that we have only played Thebes about 6 times – and we played those 6 games in about a 1 month period after our initial purchase.  Currently, we have not played this for a few months now.  While I stated some negatives in the preceding paragraph, even all of those combined would not keep us from playing it more, as we still found it fun.  I think the final thing to note – and the reason it has not been requested to play recently in our family – is that it takes a long time to play.  This in itself is not bad (for example, we enjoy rather long games of Agricola), but it *is* too long for such a simple game.  Perhaps it would speed up if we kept playing, but we were finding our games (of 3 people) to take around 2+ hours.

RATING: 6
[Initial Impression: 6] 
A light-feeling exploration game with a well-integrated theme that I think would work really well as a family game. There are probably some interesting strategic differences of rushing off to collect artifacts early and often versus waiting to build up an arsenal of knowledge and then exploring only later, but I'd need more plays to explore that strategic space. The most intriguing part of the game to me is the turn-order mechanic - doing something good for yourself moves you farther along the turn order track, so other players may then get several turns in a row before you get another turn. This means there is clearly a balance between making incremental moves and larger moves with more impact. Not a whole lot of substance to the game, but it seems fun.
(Just to show the potential impact of luck: I had 12 draws from the green bag, which contained 25 tiles: 9 good and 16 "dust", and came up with 12 dust tiles - odds of not drawing a single good tile: 3.5 in 10000!)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Thebes is an innovative light Euro, with a decent amount of luck, but also with a  certain degree of strategy to help control the luck factor.

The biggest innovation is the time system which reset turn order based on time spent in actions- the more time needed to take an action, the longer other players will have before you go again. On the other hand, a single player who has been left far behind may manage multiple turns in a row.

Many different strategies for card collection are possible based on cards available (somewhat TTR-like draw system), sites dug/un-dug, exhibitions and opportunities.  

The randomness of 'digging' for artifacts in grab bags makes the game click with the theme, and it can add attraction for less-hard strategy gamers (like families, as above). I played the 1st time with my family, came in last and had a ball.

I liked it a lot, and might like it more with additional plays; it immediately went on the short list of games to take with us on vacation.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
The claims made about Thebes are a pretzel shaped argument.  The only reason the huge luck factor is "thematic" in this game is because the game is about archaeology of a hundred years ago.  There is no way that modern archaeologists would waste their time on a dig if there was a chance they would find absolutely nothing.  Digs are based on research and analysis.  They won't know what they'll find, but they know they'll find something.  So the idea that this game is "thematic" only works if you view archaeology from long ago.  And that's just a bridge too far.  Sometimes a cigar is just a cigar.  And when it comes to Thebes, you're digging in a bag for cardboard pieces that don't depict dirt.  The card game version of Thebes, which admittedly runs far too long, mitigates some of the luck and is the far better game despite what other gamers have said about it.  The card game version is "less thematic," but that's only an issue if you bought into the theme of the board game.

RATING: 7
This game has so many elements working forit. Dripping with great flavor, it's almost an archeology sim, but a very fun one. I love the 3 'sections' of the game - research, digs, exhibits. I have heard some whining about randomness in this game, but I don't think it's applicable here. Control freak egocentrics can play Chess and Go, and stop complaining about the random. In this game, the chances you dig and find nothing are somewhat realistic. The fact two different players can have a differing range of points with the same number of tokens, so what. Even studied excavators come up empty from time to time. In all, great flavor, well-executed game mechanics, and just enough chance to make it more interesting.
