=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
遊戲設置
● 將玩家的棋子放到遊戲版圖的華沙Warsaw上。
● 每位玩家一套發掘許可證(五個顏色各一片)，及1個發掘圓盤。玩家將   他們的發掘許可證翻至無叉叉的那面。
● !!!!依遊戲人數，玩家的時間標記隨機疊在時間計表(Time track)的起點。!!!!!
2或4名玩家：圖板左上角懷錶的蓋子，數字0的位置上。
3名玩家：右上角數字16的位置上。
● 依遊戲人數，將黑色的年份標記，依玩家人數放到指定的年份上。
2或3名玩家：1901年。(2人玩三年，3人兩年半)
4名玩家：1902年。(4人玩兩年)
● 將研究卡牌覆轉洗混後，翻開4張卡牌放到圖板右上方，再將餘下卡牌平均分作3疊。
● 依遊戲人數，將博覽會卡混入分好的研究卡疊中。
2人：所有博覽會卡皆混在第2疊中，之後將第1疊放於第2疊上方。
將第3疊旁置備用，當牌疊耗盡與棄牌一同洗混作新的牌堆。
3或4人：將5張小型博覽會卡混入第2疊，5張大型博覽會卡混入第3疊之後將第1疊放於第2疊上方。
將第3疊旁置備用，當牌疊耗盡與棄牌一同洗混作新的牌堆。
!!!!!!! 在每份古蹟板塊中拿出1個1分的文物，對應顏色放到圖板上的5個發掘場地上。每份餘下共30個發掘牌，則放到同色的布袋中搖混。

遊戲流程:
!!!!!!a.玩家將依其剩下的「時間」多寡，決定行動次序。
由剩下「時間」最多的玩家執行行動，也就是時間最落後的玩家。
若剩下「時間」最多的玩家多於一人，則時間標記疊在上方者先。
圓柱標記在時間表同一格上只會疊放，後至者將疊在上方。

!!!!!!!!!!玩家執行每種行動時，皆需先計算移動時間，然後計算行動時間。棋子每沿虛線移到另一地點需花1格時間(一星期意思)。
例如從倫敦到巴黎、或柏林，即需花1格時間。
執行不同行動，玩家棋子必須移到指定的地點方可。

輪到玩家行動時，他必須進行以下行動之一，另外也不可移動但不執行動作：

1.獲取卡牌
研究卡上方印有城市名稱，玩家的棋子必須停在該城市上，玩家方可拿取此卡。
!!!!!!!!!!!!!右上角印有數字，即玩家拿取此卡時，必須支付的星期數目。
卡下方以圖案標示功用，玩家拿取後，即翻開遊戲牌堆頂的卡牌以填補空缺。
翻開的卡若是博覽會，即改放到圖板左下方。
!!!!!!!!!!圖板左下方最多只可放置3張博覽會，每有新的博覽會，所有博覽會卡將依箭頭移位一格。
多於3張博覽會卡時，丟棄移出位於C的那張博覽會卡。 
遊戲卡牌主要分為博覽會、資訊、道具、研討會。

2.撤換卡牌
玩家棋子跑到華沙Warsaw，即可多花1格時間，丟棄圖板右上方的4張卡牌，並重新翻牌填補空缺。

3.進行發掘
玩家棋子必須先跑到發掘場地，方可進行發掘，依發掘場地所屬顏色，先點算玩家的總知識點數。
玩家可從發掘圓盤找出發掘時間和抽取發掘牌數目的關係。
旋轉發掘圓盤，當最頂數字(1~12)即玩家的知識點數時，其下方數字即抽古蹟板塊的數目。
知識點數9(藍字)時，花9週(黑字)可抽9張古蹟板塊(紅字)。
!!!!!!若抽古蹟板塊的數字是0，即不可進行發掘，持有的發掘工具亦無作用。若知識點數是0，亦不可在該發掘場地進行發掘。
另外12點以上的知識點數只作12論。

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!玩家最多只可花12星期進行發掘，玩家進行發掘時，其時間標記不可跨越1903年的第52個星期。 
!!!!!!!!!!首名在某發掘場地進行發掘的玩家，可取得放在該發掘場地上的1分文物，但是抽取發掘牌數目是0者不會取得這1分文物。 
!!!!!!!!!玩家依發掘所花時間，加上發掘工具的功能後，即得出抽取古蹟板塊的數目。玩家從該發掘場地所屬的布袋中，抽取該數目的古蹟板塊後，即保有其中的文物、書本。
!!!!!其他空無一物的發掘牌則放回布袋中。

!!!!!!!玩家在每個發掘場地，1年間只可進行發掘1次。
玩家進行發掘後，將該發掘場地所屬的發掘許可證以交叉一方向上以作記錄。通過52與0之間時，即所有發掘許可證即可以翻回。

學術卡牌
a.博覽會
博覽會是橫向印刷的，左上角的數字，即此卡的所值分數。
卡下方以各色圓形表示玩家必須持有最少該數目、組合的文物方可拿取此卡。
b.資訊(知識)
分為5個文化的知識、傳言及通用知識，5個文化的專業知識以5色的書本代表，通用知識則是翻開的書本。
玩家持有卡牌上的各色書本數目，即代表其對該文化擁有多少點專業知識。
傳言則為5色的人像，在一次的挖掘可丟棄此牌以暫時增加2點該色的專業知識。
通用知識是「通用」於所有文化，但最多只會讓玩家對該文化的知識增加一倍。
舉例：玩家共有2本翻開的書本、1本紅書，則計算希臘(紅)知識時作2點知識論，其他知識仍是0點。 
c.研討會
玩家取得演說牌將為其帶來分數，玩家取得1張演說牌得1分。
但當玩家取得2張演說牌得1+2=3分；玩家取得3張演說牌得1+2+3=6分；如此類推。
!!!!但演說牌分數最多只計算7張，即玩家最多可因演說牌取得28分。

道具卡牌
a.發掘工具
當進行發掘時，玩家只有1張發掘工具，可丟棄此牌以額外多抽1發掘牌。玩家持有2張發掘工具時，進行發掘時即額外多抽1發掘牌。
玩家持有3張或以上發掘工具時，即額外多抽2發掘牌。
b.研究助手
當進行發掘時，玩家只有1張助手，可選丟棄這牌以額外多算1點專業知識。玩家持有2張助手時，進行發掘時即直接多算1點專業知識。
玩家持有3張或以上助手時，即直接多算2點專業知識。
c.飛船：當進行移動時，玩家可選丟棄持有的飛船牌，該次移動的所花時間作0計算。
d.車輛
當進行移動時，若移動的所花時間為3格以上，該玩家可少花1格時間。
e.額外許可證
丟棄額外許可證，可讓玩家在某發掘場地，無視其發掘許可證已呈交叉向上，而額外多進行一次發掘。但玩家仍必須支付移動及發掘時間。

遊戲結束
當進入遊戲的最後一年(1903)，玩家的圓柱標記踏進52，該玩家即不可再進行任何行動。當所有玩家皆無法進行任何行動時，遊戲結束。

以玩家的圓柱標記，標示各玩家的得分。
玩家各自點算其文物分數(牌上的數字)、展覽會分數、演說分數。 
玩家各自點算5色的書本數目。(助手、通用知識、傳言皆不計算)
每種顏色的書本，其中持有最多者多得5分，若多於一人時，則每人得3分
計算總分後，遊戲由分數最高者獲勝。

遊戲變體 Variant Rules

1.2或3名玩家進行遊戲時，從研究卡堆挑出3張研討會卡不使用。
2.玩家可將持有的研討會卡移出遊戲，每移出1張可扺博覽會1件文物的需求。
但上限不得超過所需文物數量的一半。(以大型博覽會而言即是3件)
3.玩家在一次的挖掘中如抽到過多的空白板塊，每6個可從研究牌堆抽1張卡作為補償。

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First play Oct 11th, 2007. It takes about 10min to be taught and can play in less than an hour for 4 players. Each are archaeologists who did up treasure (victory points). It's played over two years and each action takes a number of weeks to perform. Spares are marked in weeks. Who ever is further back (time time) goes first. To perform a dig you need a permit which you are allowed one per year unless you get a counterfit one. Special action cards are gained in cities which you must travel to and spend "weeks" gaining those items. Knoledge (books) is also obtained this way and used to increase your "digs" per site. These books are found in each site color and wild (general). There are also special cards which allows you to move in less action points and assistants which assist in digs. When diging for artifacts (pull tile from a bag) you can find dirt and thus wasted effort. Before diging you decide how long to stay and use your wheel to see how many tiles you draw. There are also extra victory points for certain collection sets of artifacts. It's a great game with a decent balance of luck and strategy. I'd recommend giving it a try if you know someone who has it and give it consideration for purchase and placing with your games to play on a regular basis.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I'm a big fan of the main mechanic in this game.  It's the same as Glenn More (a great game btw)...you essentially get to pick any avaliable action but some take more time then others allowing your opponents more turns.  For as good as the core mechanic is the digging drags this game back down.  While it may add to the theme of the game a bit it's very random.  A few poor digs can take you out of the game quickly.  To mitigate this we removed a few of the sand tokens from each bag.  This helped a bit but its still pretty random.  If you group doesn't mind the randomness then I'd say pick this game up.  Strikes me as a solid choice to play with pre-teens or young adults.  In fact, I'm surprised this game isn't dicussed more with some of the high profile gateway games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
The time keeping mechanism really makes this game stand out. A rich theme that fits well with the mechanics. There's never enough time to do what you'd like, which is where the tension lies. Yes, there's a lot of luck here, but it makes sense with the theme. Very much a game for the whole family (well, families of 4 at any rate). Playing time may run a tad long.

UPDATE: I held on to this game for quite a while until my youngest was 8 and old enough to appreciate it. When we played, it went fine. The kids liked the  bag draws, but were less enamoured of the rest of the game. I think what ends up bothering me about this game is not that there is luck, but that you spend a lot of time working to mitigate the luck and then you usually get one shot each year and even if you set yourself up well, you can still get hosed. I kind of feel like the time and investment it takes to mitigate the luck doesn't always have a satisfying payoff and I could tell that my kids felt that as well. Today I introduced them to a more recent game in a similar vein, Fool's Gold, and they were MUCH more enthusiastic about that one. I think that while luck is present in FG, the steps to mitigate that luck are more straightforward and more in your control. For that reason, I think I'm done with Thebes. Still a good game, but for us, Fool's Gold does it better. Downgraded from an 8 to a 7.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[>5 Plays]  One of my favorite Euros and probably the best integration of theme for a game with purely Euro mechanics. Luck plays a big factor in tile draws, but smart play usually (but not always) negates the effect. The time-based track is a great innovation and makes for some tough decisions on how to spend your time.

Theme: (Abstract) :star::star::star::star::star: (Lots of Theme)
Interaction: (Little Interaction) :star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar: (Lots of Interaction)
Learning Curve: (Easy to learn) :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: (Hard to learn)

[u]METRICS THAT MAY AFFECT OVERALL RATING[/u]:

Fun Factor: (I think it's no fun) :star::star::star::star::star: (I think it's LOTS of fun)
Value for the Money: (A waste) :star::star::star::star::star: (Worth it)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Incredibly fun game. Easy to learn, great connection between gameplay and theme, several ways to go about becoming a legendary archaelogist. Do you want to hire assistants, invest in more digging equipment, specialize or generalize your research, become more of an academic archaelogist or an on-site one? Do you want to go searching for more relics or exhibit the ones you already have in the galleries of Paris, Rome, Vienna, etc? There are lots of choices for players here. Probably the best thing about this game is the friendly competition in the timing: you can decide how much time you want to spend doing something, and if you invest a lot of time in a single dig, it may be a while before you get to take your next turn. By contrast, if you spend only a week or so in the library, you might get another consecutive turn. 

There is substantial luck in Thebes. Many of the choices in the game are about mitigating that luck, but don't go into this expecting good play to be rewarded above all else. I'm a heavy strategy gamer and the luck here doesn't bother me that much. I guess all I can say is that it seems appropriate and I still feel engaged in what I'm doing. 

I simply cannot think of a better family game. 

Update: I've lowered my rating for this because, despite the wonderful mechanics, I feel as though "I've seen it all before" when I play it now. Sure, there are a few different strategies, but choices are pretty obvious. I hate to say it but I'm going through the motions. Yes, the time-alottment and dig mechanics are as cool as they ever were, but now that I've "seen" it, I find very little to explore while doing it. 

It's too bad, too, because Thebes deserves a lot of credit. It's really the kind of game we all should have grown up with.

RATING: 7
Finally played the re-released version. I've originally played Jenseits von Theben years ago and, my word, the components have improved. It looks so much better now.

Players are archaelogists traversing Europe trying to either research or dig up artifacts. In comparison to the old version, the rules haven't changed at all except for exhibitions, which I think the old one was better at. Its been made more player-friendly for families most likely.

Overall I really do like this edition. Essentially the same game, but so much prettier and the theme really works for this game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Solid family friendly game with lots of flavor. Theme and game mechanic integrate exceptionally well.

One of the best turn systems I've seen.  It's based on time (weeks).  If you take an action that takes many weeks, other players will who take several small time-consuming actions can go several times before you go again.

Only player iteraction is beating them to the punch, making it a race.

Several scoring mechanisms involved give what could be very simplistic extra depth.  Unfortunately the game still relies on luck of the draw in both cards and archeological digs.  These risk can be mitigated through proper play, but can be very frustrating to watch someone draw a 7VP tile from a small time investment dig while you pull nothing but dirt from a larger investment of time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

All that said, though, it's a fun game to play, and maybe enough so to make me not care about the whole "I got a rock" issue.

RATING: 6.5
This game is a good resource (time) management game for the family, with probabilities/luck involved.  Theme is well integrated in the mechanics (luck plays a role in a dig; it's easier to find interesting stuff when you learned about the region, and when nobody has explored it yet;...).  Original turn system (a player can make plans for the 3 months ahead, but then has to wait until all the other players use these 3 months).  Simple to learn.  The scoring system is simple and makes sense.  Tactics can be relatively straightforward if you know about probabilities (getting an estimate for how much points you can get by spending your time in a given way is quite simple).  Luck can compensate for some of the difference in skills between players (but skill is useful, at least when playing against beginners).  The luck factor adds ambiance to the game ("I can't believe I found nothing in 5 weeks of digging!").  Nice board, cards, and tokens.

RATING: 4
My first play of Thebes left me lukewarm. Who would have thunk a game about going to libraries and collecting books would be fun, but I liked it despite some major shortcomings. The big aspect I liked was how time was the currency in the game. I had read about this in the past and couldn't fathom how it would work. Well it worked very well. I especially liked that turn order changed based on who was furthest back on the time line.

The short-comings of the game include the digs and the available cards, which can really screw you or help you. Digging was especially disappointing when you get a handful of dirt.

Having played the game a second time, it left me cold. I like that the game makes sense and sticks close to its theme, but there is no tension. I never think about a move for very long as choices seem very simple to me: choose a location close to where you are that reaps the greatest rewards.

Although I decry the game for being too simple, I also think it is too complex for non-gamers that are not already familiar with these types of games. Figure that one out.

[u]Result[/u]: I would rather play Arkadia, which I mention because I almost traded Arkadia for Thebes.

RATING: 7
I had been eyeing Thebes as another attractive, middle-weight game for us, particularly since it was praised as a highly thematic Euro game. We were able to get a copy in a trade and were eager to try it out.

Although the time dials are a fantastic component, the board and other markers are a little lackluster. The board in particular appeared to be pretty drab, especially considering the theme (hunting archaeological artifacts). Luckily, the game is more fun than the components would suggest and it was a good time to go travel across Europe collecting knowledge before committing to a dig. As scientists, we enjoyed the Congress and Exhibition cards (gotta go to those academic conferences to get your name out there). The time track is a nice way of determining play order and we found it amusing that there was a generally a rush to get all of your digging done in the fourth quarter at the end of the year, just like at work!

That being said, while Thebes works as a two player game and we enjoyed it, I think it would work better with more. With two, there's not quite enough tension in grabbing knowledge and in getting to a dig before the other player---with more players, there would likely be more competition for both of those. There is a great deal of luck in drawing the treasures and this may be exaggerated in the two player game. Since it's so easy to get knowledge, we both generally had a good number of pulls from the treasure bag, so it really seemed to be luck that determined who got the best treasure. Still, a nice game that we enjoyed.

RATING: 6
Although it seems highly unlikely that my rating for Thebes will go anywhere but down, I quite enjoyed my first play. "Family" games usually aren't my thing because they tend to pack 30 minutes of game into a luxurious hour, and, really, so does Thebes. Luck may account for more than 50% of a player's result, and setup's rather intricate for such a light game, too.

But, darn it, there's some cool, fun stuff in there too. While the time mechanism has been used before (and since), this was my first exposure, and it works quite nicely. The different paths to points, while layered on a little artificially, lend texture and some dimension as well. FInally, the dig mechanism, while swamped in big turns of luck, lends the game a decent sense of arc. It's the dawn of a new age of archaeology, and intrepid researchers can immortalize themselves if they make the right find. Eventually, though, sites get depleted, and great finds are rarer. It feels right, and somehow the whole package of the game doesn't feel like other games in the niche.

As usual, Queen's production values are top notch, too, aside from the ubiquitous micro-cards.

I'll be playing again in a couple of days, probably, and how well the game holds up will determine if I put this on my want list, as a digestible nod to the fans of lighter fare in my group, or if I quietly let it fade form memory. Part of me wants it to be the former.

UPDATE: Played again, and while it didn't fall apart or anything, a 7 is a bit high for this. The luck really is pretty rampant for an hour-long game. Down to a 6, which still isn't bad from me for a game in this market segment.

RATING: 8.5
MY SECRET SANTA 2013
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Thebes is a game of competitive archeology. Players are archaeologists who must travel around Europe, northern Africa, and the Middle East to acquire knowledge about five ancient civilizations -- the Greeks, the Cretans, the Egyptians, the Palestinians, and the Mesopotamians -- and then must use this knowledge to excavate historical sites in the areas of these civilizations. Through the course of the game, expositions are revealed, and an archaeologist who has treasures from the requisite civilizations may claim the prize (this is a change from the first edition's handling of exhibitions). The archaeologist who learns the most about the civilizations, claims the greatest-valued artifacts, and collects the most exhibitions will win out over his or her colleagues.
The key element to the game is that it is played out over a period of two (or three) years, and each action a player performs takes a certain amount of time -- traveling is a week between cities, gathering knowledge takes time for the level of the knowledge, and actually digging at a cultural site takes time to yield a certain number of artifact tiles. The game uses a novel mechanism to keep track of this. There is a track of 52 spaces around the outside of the board. Each time a player moves and takes an action, he or she moves their player token forward in time. Players take turns based on being the one who is furthest back in "time." So, a player can go to an excavation site and spend 10 weeks digging for artifacts, but that will also mean that the other players will likely be taking several actions in the interim while that player waits for the "time" to catch up.
In addition, the artifact tiles for each civilization are drawn from a bag that also contains dirt. When a player excavates a site, that player pulls tiles from the bag, but some may only be worthless dirt instead of valuable treasure. That dirt is then returned to the bag, making the first draw more likely to provide useful tiles.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
My wife and I have played only one game so far.  On the plus side, the time-investment process works very well - this is the first I have seen of this.  Also this game is very much on-theme.  On the minus side, there seems to be too much chance effects.  Sure you can always act so as to maximize your probabilities, but when the chance effects of cards combine with the chance effects of drawing worthless sand or very variable artifacts from the bags, it has the effect of feeling too much like flipping a coin.  It's hard to get really excited.  Also given the amount of points from various sources, it would be good if you were tracking the scores (except for majority in local knowledge) as you went so you would be more involved trying to catch up or stay ahead.  It would be nice if the artifacts found could be utilized in a more challenging way than the exhibitions.  For strategy we tried to get to the sites fairly early so as to get that first bonus artifact and the fact that the later you go to a site, the more sand there is proportionally.  Later as we built up general and specific knowledge, we could use the extra tries since there were fewer artifacts.

RATING: 8
Great fun.  Very nice components, and marvelous theme integration.  Kudos to Queen for the graphics on the cards, which make the game easy to play and teach.

Some have bemoaned the luck inherent in the tile draws for archeological digs.  I've had my share of good and bad luck in this game, and personally find the capriciousness of the tile draws thematically appropriate.  It's entirely possible to prepare wonderfully for a dig, spend plenty of time, and still find mostly damnable dust.  I should know, I AM an archeologist!

OK, not really, but I have played this game several times, and as always the luck works itself out multiple plays.  And there are enough other ways to score points to stay in the game and even win despite your rotten digging luck.  And it plays quickly, so you won't have to spend too long contemplating why the tile bags hate you.

One of my kids' favorites.

RATING: 5
3 / 5 - Good.

Well done theme off simply rules and nicely blended luck and strategy - I particularly like the balance between wanting to dig as early as possible and needing to collect knowledge and resources in order to dig. However, there is an awful lot of luck involved - as much as whether you turn over new cards in your current location as drawing from the dig bag. Wouldn't be a problem in itself, but it seems to generate very little tension or excitement. It's an unfortunate fact that all the excitement happens at the start of the game when you're racing to collect cards and there's still lots of treasures in the dig bags - late in the game there's nothing left to do and the bags are near-empty so the endgame falls flat. Works fantastically well as a family/gateway game - it's simple, enough luck for non-gamers and kids do find drawing tokens very exciting.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Game in a Nutshell: Play as archeologists racing against time and each other time to do research and conduct digs across the map in order to gain the most notoriety (victory points). 

Played as: 3,4 player

Pros: Excellent presentation of board and bits. I realize when you read a brief description of this game, there’s a sense of "yeah right, it’s probably another cardboard pusher in some drab pasted-on middle-east setting," but this really does give you a strong sense of that theme. The "time, as a currency, for what you do on your turn" element really translates well as players juggle the benefits of doing more research in order to allow them to work more efficiently in digs vs just getting out there and try to grab stuff before things get picked over. Underneath, is still a strong euro game that gives players several different avenues for scoring points whether by being more knowledgeable, spending more time lobbying congress, or putting on exhibitions with what you’ve managed to dig up. 

Cons: Some people may not like the randomness of blindly pulling tiles out of a bag representing the actual dig. It can be frustrating at times to spend a significant chunk of time digging and come up with only a couple trinkets. I’ve found this usually balances out enough to not detract, but control freaks should steer clear. I felt like there was possibly a few too many stupid little nuance rules to keep track of, with no good aid to remind you (ex: which cards do you keep vs. discard after using), for being a game on the slightly lighter side. Also, I think the game would see a lot more table time if it played about 30 minutes shorter.

Other:  In the same way RA encouraged me to try more auction games, this game makes me want to try other archeology games. With RA, my expanding in the genre just made me realize I started with the best, and I’m guessing the same might be true for Thebes and archeology games.  I think they just nailed it.

Best Use: A lighter, more ‘fun’ Euro that still has some bones. I’d probably introduce it to gamers just past the gateways.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Es un juego de unos 45 minutos-1 hora y cuyos componentes son una auténtica preciosidad. El juego es bonito de narices y la temática me encantaba, aunque este tipo de cosas, en los juegos suele ser más un “corta y pega”.
Y es que en Más allá de Tebas, cada jugador es un erudito arqueólogo que se pasa los días recorriendo las bibliotecas más importantes de Europa en búsqueda de información y pistas sobre los más importantes yacimientos arqueológicos de Egipto, Grecia... Tebas. Cuando cree haber recopilado suficientes pistas puede viajar a estos lugares e invertir las semanas que crea necesario en excavar. Puede que tenga suerte y encuentre los tesoros más valiosos, otros no tanto, pistas sobre otros posibles yacimientos o simplemente nada. El factor suerte está muy presente en este juego, pero cuantas más pistas y semanas dediques a estudiar un yacimiento, más posibilidades hay que de que encuentres algo.
Si bueno, esa es la ambientación pero, ¿realmente el juego da la sensación de que estés haciendo algo así? Las ambientaciones de muchos juegos son temas pegados de mala manera. Mira sino el Exploradores. La temática es casi idéntica pero cuando empiezas a jugar dices... ¡Si me dices que lo que estoy haciendo es cocer un buen plato de fabada, paso a paso, también me lo creo: después del 3, el 4, y luego el 5, que el 2 no lo puedo jugar porque he tapado el hueco!
Pues en Tebas si que da la sensación que realmente estás recabando pistas e investigando sobre tu próxima investigación: hay 4 cartas bocarriba que te dicen que hay 2 pistas en Viena para excavar en Egipto, 1 en Berlín para ir a Tebas, una conferencia (puntos extra) en Moscú y una pista general en Londres. Tú decides a qué ciudad viajar. Por cada ciudad que pases es una semana de tiempo, más el tiempo (siempre en semanas) que gastes en realizar la carta elegida. Mueves tu contador tantos puntos como semanas invertidas y entonces sigue el jugador que está último en el contador. Este sistema es muy ameno, bastante real y permite que si haces cosas muy pequeñas, puedas hacer varios turnos seguidos. En cambio, si decides invertir mucho tiempo en una cosa, hasta que no vuelvas a ser último en la línea de tiempo te comes los mocos.
En un momento dado, cuando crees que has recabado suficientes pistas para viajar a un yacimiento, en tu turno gastas las semanas pertinentes en llegar al lugar y en tu brújula, consultas cuantas fichas puedes sacar del saco de esa excavación según las pistas que tengas y las semanas extras que inviertas. Cuando ya lo has decidido, a lo mejor te toca sacar 7 fichas del saquito de Egipto. Algunas fichas serán tesoros, o alguna pista para otras excavaciones, y otras, simplemente arena del desierto.
Cuando han pasado 3 años (2 años con 4 jugadores), el juego termina y se recuentan los puntos de victoria (tesoros, exposiciones, mayoría de pistas en una excavación...).
El juego: me encantó. Bonito, rápido, emocionante, con algo de puteo y azar y sencillo.

RATING: 7

Investing a large amount of time near the beginning of the game to draw 7 tiles of dirt is painful - and irrecoverable.

RATING: 7
Good family-friendly game of archeology. The luck of the draw can be a tad nasty, though, so if you're easily frustrated, watch out. The game looks gorgeous, though, and though the rule book is a bit hazy on some points, this is a fairly easy and intuitive game. The time mechanism is excellent.

I've played the game with two and four, and I think I somewhat prefer the two-player game: there's more time to do research and you can plan on longer term. With four players, you get more competition. The table talk can be delicious, if your fellow players can take some ribbing and won't explode if you deride their bad luck on excavations...

All in all, it's a fun, well-themed game with some fine mechanics and a heavy dose of luck (which can be tuned a bit).

Suggest.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very thematic time management, set collection, tableau building Euro where you play as archaeologists drafting cards to gain knowledge in different colors to allow you to get the most out of a "dig" when the time is right. The "digging" aspect is controversial among my gaming group since there are so many blanks in the bag when you're finally ready to dig, that it can feel like a luckfest and a waste of time. I think the game doesn't need to be taken that seriously and even if you get a bad draw in one dig, you might get a better draw from another dig. The trick is to play the probabilities and not dig until you're ready to dig as much as possible for the best chance at getting something good. I also love the Tokaido style turn order where last place on the time track goes next so taking smaller actions nets you more turns. The flipside is that when you do a dig, you will likely spend a lot of time points and end up waiting a while for your turn. Again, the trick is to take that action after others have done it so that you don't get too far ahead of the other players and end up waiting for your turn forever.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I was really surprised by Thebes. Queen Games tend to make products where a central mechanism is used to facilitate a gaming experience, but this is one of the few cases where it was done so successfully. I was pretty weary about trying Thebes, mainly because it seemed like a game of pulling chits out of a bag, but it's so much more than that. You have to spend time preparing for your expeditions by getting knowledge, tools, and help from various cities, and then when you go, you effectively push your luck to try and find the most valuable artifacts. The drawing from a bag can feel random, but you do know the odds ahead of time. I also really like the fact that you can spend as much or as little time as you want digging based on how prepared you are.

This is just a really cool game that is surprisingly thematic for what it is. I've seen people get screwed by the bag draw, but you have to know that this element is present when you play the game. I think that as long as you play intelligently, you'll come away from this game feeling like you were competitive. One of the best games Queen has ever put out, to be sure.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
What I like best with Thebes is the time mechanism. You simply decide how much time to spend on your actions - the more time you spend, the better the outcome, but this also puts you "ahead in time", giving the turn to the other players and giving you less time to do other things. The time is spent traveling, acquiring knowledge and searching for artifacts. The more knowledgeable you are and the more time spent searching, the more tiles you get to draw from the artifact bag. The drawing may add too much randomness to Thebes but it's still a fun and light game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In 1900, each player plays an archealogical explorer (cue the Indiana Jones music). With an allotment of 52 weeks, each player spends time travelling from city to city to gain knowledge in cities where treasures are located. Treasure is represented by drawing tokens out of a bag. Typically a treasure bag would contain VP chits from 1 to 7 VPs, and half the tokens would be worthless. A player invests time and knowledge to gain a number of pulls out of the treasure bag. After a dig, the worthless tokens are thrown back into the treasure bag. 

There is a lot of luck in pulling tokens out of the bag. The game has nice components, and a lot of people don't seem to mind the luck factor. There are different strategies to try, such as collecting congress cards, or concentrating digs in only one city, but the luck appears to override anything else. Only played once with 4 players, and everyone else did not really enjoy the experience.

RATING: 10
Easy to teach, family friendly.  Very satisfying strategy/luck ratio and tactile experience makes it fun, and the theme/setting leads to some geography/history conversations with the kids - as well as the importance of goal setting ("You want to excavate in Crete?  Well, you better take that class in London and maybe grab that assistant in Rome to help you!").  

For adults, the gameplay has a few different paths to success, some "take that", and push your luck elements.  And while luck is a factor, you can mitigate it - and you always know your odds.  Works well as 2 player game, though 3 and 4 player also fun.  Turns are quick.  All in all a great game at an accessible price (and very good quality components and nice artwork).

RATING: 6
Interesting game that has lots of luck embedded into it, although it is one of those luck-driven games where as long as you intelligently play the odds, you are ahead of the game.

You play an Indiana Jones type archaeologist that is traveling across Europe to uncover secret artifacts that will eventually score you points and win the game.  You can only dig at one of the five types of sites once per game, so you do not want to jump in one too early without enough 'intelligence points', however, if you wait long enough someone else might dig there first and steal all the good treasure.

A decent push-your-luck set collecting game.  I fine this to be a bit of a mix of Ticket to Ride and hmmm...a game that has a luck based system to it...

RATING: 4
210517
Played with 6 year old. This is a point salad but not in a good way. The main way of getting points is through digging for artifacts but the randomness of the draw really makes planning very difficult. There is hardly any strategy here and most of the time, gameplay is based on tactical decisions of where to go based on which cards are on offer. Most of the time, you'll be maximising the weeks spent by heading to a location with at least 2 cards to take. 

The decision on how many weeks to spend digging for treasure is also not satisfying as you don't want to spend too many weeks lest your opponent gets too many turns but you also need to balance the probability of digging up treasures based on what's left. Most of the time we end up choosing taking 6 weeks as it's the median of what's available to choose from. 

In the end, I won by a huge margin but I had no idea how who was ahead during the game as we didn't keep track of our artifact scores and there was no game provided means to do so short of using pen and paper. I think this is what brings the game down for me.

RATING: 7
心得:
運氣很重要的容易上手遊戲
但是還是有些風險管理跟策略規劃
規則:
1.先抽四張牌擺圖版，將剩餘牌分成三堆，將小展覽洗進第二堆，大展覽洗進第三推，之後第一第二疊起來，兩人遊戲則全部洗進第二堆
2.2p : 1901起始0, 3p : 1901起始16, 4p : 1902起始0 
3.車卡 一棟超過三周可以少一周 ，飛船卡用完即丟棄
4.在華沙可以花一周把四張牌清掉，連續清的話，第二次要兩周，以此類推
5.每回一定要做動作,a拿卡b清卡c挖掘d開展覽
6.當第一二疊的牌堆用完，將棄卡與第三推洗好作為新的牌庫
7.最後記分，文物版塊，展覽卡，會議卡(最多28)，各顏色知識第一名五分，多人平手則各得三分
8.
A.2或3名玩家進行遊戲時，從研究卡堆挑出3張研討會卡不使用。
B.玩家可將持有的研討會卡移出遊戲，每移出1張可扺博覽會1件文物的需求。
但上限不得超過所需文物數量的一半。(以大型博覽會而言即是3件)
C.玩家在一次的挖掘中如抽到過多的空白板塊，每6個可從研究牌堆抽1張卡作為補償。
9.BGG變體規則
a每次挖掘後拿掉一個土(不管該次有沒挖到土)
b每次遊戲可選擇花兩天全部重抽
c上限車1助手跟鏟子3 助手鏟子如果沒上限，多的算第二組
d可棄專業知識卡，花一周，在多抽卡上書本量的版塊
e限制單次挖掘分數上限8，或可花兩周重挖
f小展覽 5分兩周 大展覽 8分三周
g每種顏色一個 五個一組 2分

RATING: 3
I think it's too luck based
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Delivering efficient and fun Euros, Queen games are usually "lean," using innovative and simple mechanics. And Thebes is no exception. Players are archaeologists spending most of the game preparing for their "digs" at ancient sites to find artifacts to bring back and sell back in Europe for the most fame. Here, time, not money or food is the main commodity of the game: every action requires time and the player who best utilizes theirs to find the most valuable artifacts wins. I love the "digging"/push-your-luck mechanic of the game because it is reliant on randomness. And players can spend more time at dig sites to draw more from the bag or gather cards that will improve chances. Every "dig site" is a bag of tokens with roughly a 50% chance of drawing something of value; the other half are blank tokens, or "debris." And no matter the outcome of your dig, debris tokens are added back to the bag to lower the chances of drawing something of value. As bags get depleted of their value, players scramble to other "dig sites" for better artifacts. The tension of drawing from the bag made the game much more enjoyable than a straight strategy game for me. Paulo beat me in the end, only because if you have the most points at one site you get endgame bonuses. Overall, a fantastic game where players balance luck and strategy for the most points. While that sounds like every Euro, Thebes will always get played when I'm around because it's fast and easy to understand.

RATING: 8
"Very thematic game with great mechanisms. Great as a 'next-step' game after gateways, but interesting for more serious gamers who are OK with some randomness."

What I like:
- LOVE the time-track mechanism
- Beautiful components
- Every aspect of the game fits the theme well
- Plenty of strategic and tactical decisions

What I don't like:
- Sometimes the chit draws can be swingy
- Can run long (if people don't move briskly)

It's on the complicated side for non-gamers (the 2.2 weight is probably a bit low--I would say 2.4), but some serious gamers are going to hate the chit-drawing (and card-drawing) randomness (and might overlook the strategies for mitigating the luck).

I have played 2p games in as little as 45 minutes, but a 4p learning game took 150. Probably 90-120 minutes is a reasonable target for most player counts, with more players toward the upper end. It is best played with "instinctive" players rather than "analytical" players, to keep the pace up.

RATING: 8
A fun game about being an archaeologist digging up treasures from the ancient world.  The use of time as both the "clock" for the game itself and a way to set turn order is interesting.  And the digging aspects and execution are very neat.

Many will complain about the sheer randomness of getting treasures out of digs, particularly after a given location has been the subject of a number of digs.  But this seems to fit the theme quite well - as you dig up stuff, there's less stuff to find.

Lots of interesting choices to make here, good strategy, and a number of different paths to victory make this a good game.

RATING: 8
Thebes is unusual in the sense that it has very strong theme: Turn of the century archaeological expeditions by Europeans in the Mid and Near East.  The main facets of this activity are resource development/study, expeditions to dig up artifacts, and exhibitions to show them off for fame.  The comment complaint on expeditions is the influence of luck -- so be it...I personally enjoy the way it is done.

My one complaint with this edition is that the selection of exhibitions are arbitrary, in that certain combinations of artifacts allow for no exhibitions to be held for them.  It seems unnecessarily serendipitous.

RATING: 8
More thematically filling than most euros, and easy to explain as everything makes sense.  Has a nice length to it, and there are multiple options for scoring.  Has a couple cute mechanics and rewards patience and doesn't feel completely pressured to do more than you want (like, say, Through the Desert or Railroad Tycoon).  Fits a nice, newbie friendly niche, but isn't totally reliant on strategy, and AP is pretty much out the window (as I said, there are multiple decisions to make, sometimes obvious, sometimes a little more subtle, but all are logically easy to grasp).  Plus, the disks are fun to use.  Has a push-your-luck element in the digs of time vs. # of chips to draw, which I enjoy.

Though some may complain that there's no player interaction, there is a "step on somebody's toes" element, where you take things before others do, and this can sometimes lead to strategy choices based on card distribution and the number of dig chips taken.  Also, you tend to have time to react to other people's (apparent) long-term strategies.

That said, I'm not sure how much I enjoy it.  I'm not sure it really scratches my nerdy competition itch, and the chip draw can be obnoxiously lucky at times.  Still my first game was fun and close, and I look forward to at least the next few plays, while likely to suggest it, so it gets an 8.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Thebes is the kind of game that can get the whole family excited, and not in a Disney kind of way.. and that's good.  That's good, because it means that Thebes is a game that can be played with good friends who may/may not be 'all that' into gaming.

For me, my game groups tend to fall in both categories and so Thebes does very well and I expect it to do so well in to the future.

Thebes, in many ways, mimics a kind of 'Indiana Jones' simplicity into early 20th century archeology.  In this game, players will move from city to city to gain knowledge of various dig sites.

Once acquired, players take that knowledge to the dig site and determine how much time they want to invest in looking for artifacts.. and that's how the game is measured:  time.  Around the board are numbers 1-52, representing weeks of the year.  As weeks become months and months become years, players will gain more artifacts, present more exhibitions (courtesy of the artifacts), and gain more notoriety.  

In the end, and as per usual, whoever has the most points wins.

There's the synopsis.  The game, though, is quite simple and easy to grasp.  There is some strategy in choosing what your path may be to victory (ie: what site you want to dig in), and there's some fun and excitement to be wrung from drawing tokens at random from one of the 5 provided 'dig bags' in corresponding colour.

Lots of fun, worth an 8, re-playability should be OK, but yet luck plays a massive factor (via cards and random draws) that anyone can beat pretty much anyone else, if luck plays a major factor at all.

A fun game, despite the element of luck. The theme works well and the beautiful components/board make this game. The meshing of Euro elements with the luck of 'digging' artifact chits from cloth bags just seems to work somehow (even though, on paper it doesn't seem like it would). Sometimes a lucky player will win over someone who has played more tactically (but that seems to fit the theme, too). It is fun to chant 'dirt, dirt, dirt' as someone else pulls nothing but dirt during one of their excavations (and then angrily throws the bag across the room).

Rating changed from 8.7 - 6/24/2001

RATING: 1
The luck factor in this game is extremely high. The downtime between turns is also extremely high without giving you much if anything to think about. The game is incredibly mathy as you have to budget 52 action points per round. The cards also awkwardly don't have consistent iconography which means cards will behave differently from how you would suspect, which really hurts as they all have really basic effects. I hope to not have to play this game again.

:nostar: Art, Iconography and components functional
:nostar: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:nostar: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:nostar: Multiple plays stay interesting
:nostar: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 8
The game is well interwoven with thematic mechanisms.
I like the time based turn mechanic. The actions take a variable amount of time, which drives the turn order and when you can take the next turn. 
Discovering is by digging into the artifact bag to pull tiles. Each of the sites have a slightly different set of tile values.
Collecting sets of knowledge help your chances of finding something, You get to pull more tiles. Very important in the early phases.  

Good family style game with a nice bit of strategy and a healthy dollop of randomness.

RATING: 7.5
Thebes is probably one of my game where the theme is the most relevent. Easy machanics, and more than average material quality makes it one of my favorite queen's game. There is a fare share of luck in the game that can leave strategy fan frustrated. But I thinks that the luck factor makes it accessible for all because you may have the best strategy and still loose if your unlucky at the draw. The lurning curve is rapid so everyone can play easely. The digging process realy reflect the frustration that archeologist must face, and are still facing now after unsuccessfull digs. The time track and chronograph to determine the amount of weeks per digs are very intersting and make thebes stand up  alone in it's class.

Thebes est probablement un de mes jeux ou le thème est le plus présent. Une mécanique simple et du matériel de qualité supérieur en font l'un de mes jeux de Queen games favorit. Il y a une bonne part de chance dans ce jeu ce qui peut frustrer les adeptes de jeux de stratégie. Mais je crois que le facteur chance en fait un jeu accessible à tous car vous pouvez avoir la meilleur stratégie et perdre du à la malchance lors des fouilles. La courbe d'apprentissage est rapide et le jeux peut être enseigné en un rien de temps. Le processus de fouille représente bien la frustration des archéologues face à des fouilles infructueuses. La piste de marquage du temps ainsi que les chronographes pour déterminer le temps alloués pour les fouilles sont des éléments intéressants qui font de Thebes un jeu qui se trouve dans une classe à part

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The way that time is used as money to be spent is both innovative and fun.  I love that a person who takes one big turn can still get as much from their game play as a person who takes a number of little turns and it's also neat that the person who chooses to take a bunch of little turns can also get an advantage.  Thebes is neat because it forces players to choose the right time to make their big moves.  

Many people say that they don't like Thebes because there is too much luck.  There is a lot of luck in Thebes.  However, there is a lot to be said for using the time mechanic to get the most out of your game play and to maximize your excavation pulls. Also, a player who is clever about take a lot of short turns at the right time can also gain a huge advantage.  

Thebes is definitely a game of huge player interactions.  Everything one player does affects the other players.  Of course, I wouldn't expect anything else from a game that is about archeology.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Clever archaeology game that uses a time mechanic for chosen actions.
Gain knowledge in the different historical dig-sites, get helpers and listen to rumour. When you feel ready then begin to dig. 
How long you dig for determines how many 'finds' you make. Will it be treasure or just rubble?
Spend time to exhibit your finds to earn extra points.
Plays very well, some luck but that's just like archaeology itself!
Good fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game almost feels like an Amerigame rather than a Euro: it's luck-heavy and more thematic than usual for Euros. This new edition has much improved bits (the excavation wheels that replace the charts, the tokens and draw bags that replace the endlessly-reshuffled card decks), excellent artwork, and a summary sheet that identifies all the archaeological artifacts that appear on the tokens. Although much depends on your luck in being in the right place at the right time, and in pulling the right tokens from the bags, there is still some strategy in the game and you have some control over how far you will push your luck. The mechanism of spending "weeks" to get things done, with the player who has spent the least time always taking the next turn, is great.

Best with two or three players. With four, the exhibitions are nearly useless because there's too much competition for the digs.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Thebes works very well for myself and my family.  I enjoy the fact that Thebes' mechanics and theme work so well together.  The time track mechanism generates some neat decision points in the game (Do I dig a long time hoping to turn up treasures or spend my time in shorter increments to pick up more cards and score via other mechanisms?)

The components are nice, although I would have liked some larger cards (admittedly, I have huge hands so maybe thats a "me" problem :) ).  There are also 5 bags that you draw tokens from.  

Drawing tokens from the bag is great fun for my kids, and its fun for me as well.  Yes, its heavily luck influenced but who cares - its fun, and it again fits the theme (maybe you get lucky and uncover a great find, maybe you don't).  If you would prefer a little more control over your destiny, then work on collecting congress cards and try to be the most knowledgable in several areas of study.  A lot of points can be scored in this manner if everyone else is a little too dig happy.  Of course, then you miss out on all the fun drawing tokens out of the bag :D.

RATING: 8
A deep game with much planning and strategy. Turn order based on which player has used the least "time points" works very well -- great innovation by Prinz that has been copied by others (including Wallace, in Tinners Trail). Digging for relics in a tile bag filled with some zero value tiles adds randomness and fits the theme perfectly. Missions and limited movement each turn makes this a tense and highly interactive game.

RATING: 8
This is a fun game with plenty of theme.  Ignore the issues or "fixes" concerning the random artifact draw; that is what makes this game fun.  Everything else is perfect information...the only thing that you DON'T know is what you are going to draw out of that bag.  But since you can see everything that everyone else has drawn, and there are cards that outline what is available at each dig site, you can use the power of probability to make the best decision as far as location and amount of time to dig. The time mechanic is also unique and thematic. A lot of things come together to make this a fun game that is quite underrated.

RATING: 6.9
Winning this game is secondary to playing and experiencing it. Maneuvering around the map, collecting the various cards, and the idea of the excavations themselves all make a very thematic and fun game to play. Using time as both the resource pool for actions and the game timer, is very clever, and managing time spent versus efficiency makes for consistently challenging choices. While the winner of this game is largely randomly determined by who drew better chits from bag, the fun comes from the balancing act of building yourself up for big excavations- with the hope that you'll be able to get a lot of treasure- and managing your time to be able to do other things as well.

RATING: 5
Not sure what to think about this one. On one hand, it's got some great mechanics, but I think the intense luck element makes for a completely unpredictable experience. 

The components are top-notch with an all-out production value you would expect from a Queen game. The typical score track around the board edge is not  a score track, but a time tracker. This was my favorite element from the game, because it creates some tense decisions. Drawing chits from the bag does, in a way, emulate an archeological dig. In other words, the theme fits the mechanics.

My problem with the game: You're slammed with a double dose of luck. The first is with the cards, the second is with the dig site tokens. There's no way of knowing where certain cards will turn up, and there's certainly no predictability in the tile draws. It turns into a frantic and chaotic game of reacting to all the luck.

It seems like this game would be valuable as a gateway game, but I want something with a little more depth and planning.

RATING: 8.6
Two Player Value = Excellent 

Three Player Value = Excellent 


Note - I have played the original [thing=13883][/thing].  For this writing I will refer to this version as being the first to use these game mechanics for two reasons.  One, the overall mechanics remain the same.  Two, this version is much more available.


Thebes is a fantastic game is just about every way imaginable.  In fact, it is difficult to say a single bad thing about it.

For starters, the game is simply beautiful.  This is truly a lovely game.  Every piece has brilliant artwork.  Even the draw bags are color-coded and well made.

But the wonder of this game is the new and inventive game play.  This game is unique in severals ways.  It is this beauty in design that makes this game really shine.

Thebes is so unique that I can only try to describe the gameplay.  It uses a timed drafting mechanic for set collection.  Then players use the drafted sets for timed blind draw.  It sounds very odd, but plays fantastically.

This was the first game to use the time track as the action selection timer.  This is a brilliant innovation and forces players into tough decisions.  Yet it remains simple and elegant.  It is easy to teach, learn and play.

The theme is integral to the game play.  You get a fantastically rushed feeling as timing becomes extremely important and critically tight.

The rules are written well.  Learning the game from the rulebook is easy.

Honestly, I cannot say one bad thing about this game.   It is wonderful in every way.  This game belongs in every game collection.  






Pros - 
- Brilliant design 
- Beautiful 
- Unique mechanics
- Easy to learn
- Great for all level of gamers


Cons - None



Overall Playability = 8.6

Design = 9
Strategy = 9
Luck = 8
Artwork & Components = 9
Use of Mechanics = 9
Time of Play = 8
Fun = 9
Innovation = 10
Analysis Paralysis = 7
Theme = 8

[b]Unique Mechanic[/b] - The Time Track is extremely inventive.  This is a great way to judge player turns.

RATING: 7
Fun, young-person-friendly game. In spite of lacking much in the way of real strategic depth, the time management mechanic and the (thematic) fun of the chance-laden artefact draws combine to make play engaging. 

I think this is a neat combination of euro and AT sensibility : the theme is great, and although chance will play a big part in determining the winner, decisions feel real and meaningful throughout the game. 

Furthermore, if you're in any way a 'fan' of 19th century cultural imperialism, well, this game really hits the spot. 

Should be sold in museums everywhere instead of the lame 'roll and move' themed games they always carry in the gift shops - except then it would have to be called "The Crazy Fun Treasure Quest Game!"  

Very nice components (archaeological drawbags and Indymeeples) and very nicely priced too. Rulebook could be a bit clearer on certain points, although after a play and a re-read it's all pretty much common sense. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Queen Games version. Much loved by the family with many groans and smiles depending upon the success of the digs. Very thematic - I really dig the digging out of the bags for the artefacts. The turn order is a much liked mechanism.. I've played this many times and look forward to each time it makes it to the table, but I don't find myself ever suggesting it. I have to engage a certain mindset when playing that even the best laid plans will be at the mercy of the sieve and shovel.

What prevents me from enjoying this more is the large impact the luck of the dig has on the scores. Maybe the luck of archaeology is just not for me? I've seen one player remove 20 points from the first dig of a bag (with eight total tokens), and another player remove three points with the first pull of the bag (also with eight tokens). This leaves a somewhat sour taste I assure you. (Update: first pull - one treasure from 11 tokens!) I'm wondering whether the distribution of points should be a little flatter, with the number of total points in the bag remaining the same as it currently is. There would still be the excitement of pulling (say) four points from the bag with a small dig of a token, but it would reduce the luck element to a more tolerable level in my opinion. A balanced game seems best, with smaller digs to leave yourself able to jump around and get opportune cards as they come up. That said, nothing seems to work as well as just pulling the best tiles from the bag.

VARIANT: Each zero blank dig chip is worth half a point (rounded up), and is still returned to the bag. All other chips are worth the same. Yes this affects the balance of digs v exhibitions, but it makes for a more satisfying game. Yes this screws up the theme knowing that when you dig four chips you'll get at least two points, but it makes for a more satisfying game.

RATING: 6
Thebes is a fun game, but the luck of the draw in Excavations is increasingly disappointing.

I have a fix for that, though I haven't tried it yet. Ideally, doing more research will get you a better chance to get more VPs when digging... but currently it's wildly erratic as to how many points you get. One player could dig for 5 weeks being not-all-that prepared and get 4 treasures, while another player could have 12 knowledge and dig for 10 weeks and get 1/2 as many points. My group has found that [i]highly[/i] unsatisfying. Here's my suggested fix to make this otherwise very fun game more interesting and desirable to play...

When you dig, you only keep 1 Treasure. If you go in with a lot of research, maybe you keep 2. The more tiles you draw, the better your chances of drawing the high value treasures, so it still pays to be prepared, but if you're lucky vs unlucky, it's only a 6 point swing, not 20 . Ideally, the dial numbers would change a bit, and maybe show 2 values (the number of tiles to dig, and the number of treasures to keep), but since the game's already been produced, here's the Ghetto fix:

When you dig for 1-6 weeks, you keep a maximum of 1 Treasure (as well as any books you find). When you dig for 7-12 weeks, you keep a maximum of 2 treasures (as well as any books you find). Shovels allow you to keep 1 additional Treasure rather than draw an additional tile (or maybe both).

It may be that some of the sand in each bag should be removed in that case... probably about 6 of them. Or maybe not... not sure about that.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very fun gateway game with great thematic immersion. The game has an elegant time track design that helps rein in runaway leaders, and introduces agonizing decisions on how much time to spend on digs and other actions. Very tight game design that makes the game just right for the amount of chaos and randomness involved. Doesn't outstay its welcome.

--------------------

My opinion of the game has dimmed a little because you can't manipulate the luck at all when you commence the dig. The capriciousness of luck in two elements: the digs and card draws, can really undermine a lot of the good strategic value found elsewhere in the game.

RATING: 4.5
Okay - back to square one. 

The reason I disliked this game: 
- Because bgg recommendation thread made me think this is a thematic game. Isn't. It's a thematic [i]euro[/i].

RATING: 8
Players are archaeologists travelling around the Greece and the near east trying to dig for valuable artifacts. These give VPs. Along the way, they can pick up equipment to help their digs.

The gameplay features innovative 'time' movement mechanics that are reminiscent of Neuland (Eggert). Players move their 'time' marker along a scale for taking actions (travel, digging, picking up equipment). 

Gripes:
There is a little AP towards the end when players are trying to calculate the best time to spend on digs. It's slightly fiddly; all the site cards are too small and clutter the table. The spread between the value 1 and 7 treasures can make or break the game.

The Queen edition ups the value of the game with the draws-string bags for pulling digs and with the fantastic dig dial! They've made a great game even better!

RATING: 6
First, I have to say everything is beautiful.  It is a sumptuous looking game.

I know that there are only a couple minor tweaks in this version, but I just didn't like it as much as the original. I feel that the original game is just more tense and the new version doesn't include some of the advanced rules of the original that mitigates much of the luck factor.  That's not to say that the original isn't a luck heavy game, but I feel that there is more of a choice as to when you should be pulling the trigger to start digging.  In the original advanced rules you don't put in all the dirt until you take out treasures.  This encourages early digs at a higher inefficiency.  Of course, this can be incorporated into the new game.

I don't mind the change from spending two weeks anywhere to refresh the draw pile to a single week in Warsaw, but I don't like that you can't see the next two upcoming cards as you were able to do in the 2nd edition.  Again this can be easily incorporated in the new version.

Other changes that I liked better in the original that can still be transported are the dependence of general knowledge on special knowledge so that you can't use more general than you have specific.  This forces more decisions and makes the game tighter.  The end game used to grant 4 points per special majority now it is 5 to add incentive rather than have the game play compel you.  By eliminating this dependence it also decreases the real need for assistants.

The main reason that I feel the need to hold on to my old version and not get this is the way the exhibits worked.  In my opinion the original exhibit system is superior, it made the game much more tense and demanding with more exciting results.  This new version rewards the player that draws more treasures.  In the other game you can race there with your crap and hope to beat someone with more booty, or just time it right so that all other players are busy.  The new system pushes exhibits out too quickly so that you may not get the chance to even compete.

The new version is fine, but it just feels more dependent on the draws for my taste.

RATING: 8.5
A great game!  This has a couple of unique mechanics that combine to make for really interesting, yet simple gameplay.  Anything that you do to help you has a cost.  The more it helps, the more expensive that it is.  The cost is not in money, but rather in opportunity, as you'll have fewer actions to take, and until other players have moved past you, you don't get to take your turn.  The theme is well-done and the components are top-notch: cloth bags, beautiful board, and pretty cardboard tokens for archaeological finds.  There certainly is some luck with regards to the success of your digs, but not to introduce too much randomness.  A truly elegant game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
A bit too true to theme for me -- I don't like the random nature of the chit pulls, although the game has a bunch of other really cool stuff: time management, turn order manipulation, pick up and deliver (sort of), quick play, beautiful production, etc. That randomness just makes me want to stay away. 

-----------
Everyone starts with their pawns in Warsaw. 4 cards are on display. The cards generally show a color of knowledge (1-3 books), but there are other types as well (shovels, assistants, etc.; deeper in the deck, exhibitions). Time markers for each player start at 0 (track goes to 52 = weeks/year; separate track to record years; 4 player game is for 2 years). On your turn, you do one of 4 things:
- take a card from display: travel to that city (1 week per leg) and take the card (paying additional weeks as indicated on the card); immediately draw replacement
- excavate: travel to 1 of the 5 sites (only allowed to dig once/site/year; 2 years in game), use the wheel to cross-reference the amount of knowledge you have in that color (= #books specialized knowledge + #books general knowledge (not more than specialized knowledge) ) with the number of weeks you want to spend ... this gives you a number; pull that number of discs (variable VP) from the like-colored bag, returning any blank ones (dirt) to the bag 
- clear the display: go to Warsaw (pay travel time), discard the cards, draw replacements
- have an exhibit: the 3 most-recently drawn exhibits are available; each shows a combination of discs (varying numbers and colors) and location; if you have that combo, travel to the location, spend the required weeks of time, and take the exhibit card = VP

The player who has spent the least time so far (furthest back on the score track) goes next. The game ends after everyone has spent exactly 2 years. The player with the most VP wins!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This is exactly what I look for in a lighter and quicker game.  I'm mostly interested in heavier games but sometimes you're playing with people who just need something that plays in under an hour and is easy to explain and grasp.  This game fits all those criteria but still has some very interesting mechanics and decisions to make and that gives it a lot of appeal in my eyes.

In this game the players play an archeologist trying to find treasure at different dig sites but in order to dig first they need knowledge of the area.  Knowledge is represented by cards that the players can purchase.  The most interesting mechanic in this game is that the main currency in this game is time.  The game ends after 2-3 years depending on the number of players and this is divided into 52 weeks per year and everything costs a certain number of weeks from traveling from area to area to "purchasing" cards.  This is where the most of the interesting decisions come in because you can choose how many weeks to dig at a site.  The more weeks you dig the better chance you have to find valuable treasure but the less other things you can do.

Also, the actual digging is represented by pulling tiles out of a bag so sometimes you get valuable treasure and other times you get useless debris...which goes back into the bag and dilutes the site for the next dig.  As I've already said, there's tons of interesting decisions to make here, it plays really quickly (a 2 player game took us around half an hour and that's including setup, tear down, and teaching the game), and is easy to catch on.  Plus there is a nice mix of strategic thinking with a healthy dose of randomness in the blind digging in the bags.

I STILL can't fall completely in love with a game this light and straightforward, I would probably get bored with it if I played it too often.  But for the right crowd/time slot there's not much better and I really respect the game for that.

RATING: 7
I really liked this game. The time track mechanic was interesting and relatively unique within my game-playing experience. (I can only think of Neuland as having a similar mechanic, but I'm sure there are others.)

The rules were straightforward and easy to grasp (of course, that might have been due to a good teacher, as I did not look at the rulebook myself). Throughout the game I felt like I always understood what was going on and could come up with an idea of what I should do next.

My main mistake was, probably predictably, misjudging how much time I would need to successfully dig for artifacts. I was the second player (out of four) who headed to the desert to dig, but the first player digging got their nearly half a year before I did and that extra time paid off for him. The mechanism of throwing the junk back into the bag was very clever, although maddening in actual play!

I would definitely like to try this one again. I think I could sell it to my wife too, which is always a plus.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[Queens Game Multi-lingual 07 edition] {Archeology, gateway; drafting, variable-player-order, luck-pressing; scalable} I got Thebes because it is highly recommended by my buddies. I am not disappointed. It is interactive, very fun and highly thematic. The time track is an original mechanic.  And the mechanics integrate well with the theme.  The random factor in the tile draw may be high but it's fun, balanced and politically correct (how can one know what they can dig from a site in advance?).  The game is medium-weighted - there are meaningful decisions, but not taxing. Besides, it's engaging - the winner is not determined until the end of game.  I like this game very much.

The components are stunning. The board is nicely illustrated.  The cards are effective and thick, though I prefer larger cards.  It's so thematic that it seems I am actually collecting artifacts from various sites. The wheel is beautiful and it's more fancy then looking a chart. In all, a very pretty presentation!

This is an excellent attempt for first-time designer.  Keep up the good work!

RATING: 7
Rating based on one play.

Wow, the luck factor is quite high, but what fun! The theme is really well integrated and actually accounts for much of the luck. For example, invest a ton of time at a dig site and you could still walk away with nothing. We found ourselves making Indiana Jones references throughout the game. Very fun.

As far as strategies, each player tried something a little different... I dug for artifacts based on which exhibitions were coming up, another player focused on lectures, while another just dug a lot in the hopes of uncovering really valuable artifacts. I love games that allow this kind of playing options.

Update: Despite the fun, the amount of luck is starting to get on my nerves. I had to drop my rating a bit.

Update: Traded away.

Last played: Jan 2008

RATING: 6
An engaging game with one one serious flaw: The luck factor is extremely high in relation to the game length, and hits both in terms of what cards are available to players AND in the excavations. High luck factors of this magnitude are a bit more palatable in quick "filler" games. The positives still outweigh the negatives here, though; the game has interesting mechanics, a ton of player options, many paths to victory and a theme that fits well.

- Game turns are innovatively divided (the score track is mainly used to indicate each players' progress - not point-wise but how far they have progressed in the game)

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

RATING: 7.5
It's a solid Euro that does fit the theme very well, which is rare.  You really do sort of feel like you've taken on the role of an Archaeologist.  It's one of the few games where the large luck dependency really doesn't bother me at all, because you're supposed to be digging in the ground, at the end of the day it is luck.

It plays pretty quick and is very easy to learn.

Queen games, as usual, provided solid production value.

The only downside is that the decisions you make are just a little bit too shallow, it's the rare game that I feel could have used an expansion or something to give just little more depth.

Great for families or for a light-hearted 60-90 minute play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Thebes is a game that I had been wanting to try for a long, long time. It was one of those games on the borderline of my wishlist that I had been tempted to pick up many times, but which I wasn’t sure whether I’d like enough to warrant owning (and the large box size kept it from being an impulse purchase given my small apartment and overcrowded shelves). I’m glad I finally had the chance to try Thebes thanks to Lisa and Brian from Colorado teaching it at BGG.CON. Unfortunately, even after playing it, I’m still on the fence about whether it’s worth owning. I liked it, but I didn’t love it nearly as much as many others that I’ve read. I wasn’t quite as bothered by the luck of drawing chits from a bag to simulate an archaeological dig as I had feared. However, I also wasn’t as overcome with the sense that it was a German-style board game that especially embodied its theme. The time track is a very neat idea and one that’s used to great effect in both Thebes and Red November. I expect to see more games in 2009 that employ this mechanic, and perhaps a whole genre of time track games develop (like the recent outbreaks of worker placement games, cooperative games, and racing games). I’m tempted to pick-up Thebes, but I also fear that it might be caught in the no-man’s middle ground between gateway game and more complex games, just like Thurn & Taxis (although certainly not as boring as Thurn & Taxis). It’s certainly more complicated than Ticket to Ride and Carcassonne, but not a very complex game by any means. I’d like to play it again, which seems to be my mantra for many of these games I tried at BGG.CON (except Boss Kito), but shelf space is at too high a premium to fit Thebes for the time being.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I knew a few things about Thebes going into it... 1) it had an interesting time track/turn order mechanic 2) that you are archaeologists "digging" for treasure by drawing tiles out of a bag 3) that two people I know refuse to play it.

As mentioned, the key mechanism of the game is that doing things take time. Travelling to cities requires 1 time (a week) for each path you have to travel between spots. Additionally picking up items in places costs times, as does displaying an exhibit in an exhibition, or digging in a dig site. The player farthest behind on the time track is the next player to move. So if you spend a lot of time doing something, it'll be a while before it is your turn again. I love this mechanism.

The game revolves around digging and preparing to dig. You spend a lot of time picking up books that give you better odds of digging at particular locations. There's also books that help you at all locations, but you can only use those up to the point that you match the books you have for a particular place. There's also shovels and grad students that help you dig. When you go to dig, you consult a table (in convenient dial format) that tells you based on your book count, how many tiles you draw if you devote x weeks to the dig. So you decide how long you'll dig, and then draw your tiles. There's something like 30 tiles in a bag, half of which are blank, with the rest ranging in value from 1 to 5 (and in one or two rare cases 6 or 7). And while you remove the point tiles when you find them, all the "dirt" blank tiles return to the bag.

And that's where the issue with the game raises its ugly head and why I know two people who refuse to play this game. Because it's entirely possible for a player to draw a single time getting a 5 point tile, while you draw 11 times and either don't get a tile, or get a 1 point tile.

In our play of the game other players were doing fairly well at getting tiles. And then I tried. And things did not go well. Someone else would draw 4 tiles and get 10 points, and I'd draw 6 tiles and get 3 points. And then I'd draw 8 and only get a book and a point. Given my bad luck, I decided to try to brute force my way into points, where I'd draw 11 tiles at a time. But even then it was rough going. An opponent got 4 points drawing 3 tiles and I got 3 drawing 11. Or maybe I got the 4 and he got the 3. Either way, it was pretty frustrating.

I managed to do well in the game... but that was largely due to exhibitions and maxing out one dig site where I had a huge advantage. And again much of my exhibition score was a lucky break where I happened to qualify for 2 exhibitions (where you display the treasures you've found) that came out in the same location and I was able to swoop in and score them in a single fell. If they had come out a little sooner, or a little later, or in different locations, I couldn't have gotten both. Or if I had found 1 treasure less at the yellow dig site, I couldn't have gotten the nicer one.

So what do I think? Well... honestly. I think the game has a lot of good things going for it (especially the time track) but the randomness of the tile draw makes it problematic. There's a lot of game wrapped around that random tile draw. And while you can try to do a lot to improve your odds, it's incredibly frustrating to have spent a good chunk of time making your odds better than your opponents and then watching them get lucky while you don't. If I'm putting this much time and effort into building myself up, I don't want it to be so easily undone by luck.

Will I play again? I'm honestly not sure. I liked the game. It was fun to play. But it was really frustrating to struggle with bad luck. If the luck had been worse and I was getting nothing drawing 11 tiles consistently, then I think this would have been a miserable experience. Unfortunately, I think that could happen.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[thing=30869][/thing] is a point to point movement, management of time and using the probabilities in order to take the most of excavations, while expecting the least from luck.

The flow of the play is simple: players move their archaeologists through Europe in order to collect knowledge (specific or general), rumors, assistents, etc. Then they go to the archaelogic sites - Greece, Creta, Palestine, Egypt and Iraq (Mesopotamia) - where they will put to use the informations in order to have better chances, for less time, to find the valuable itens instead of worthless rubble.

The twist is the use of time: all actions - moving, taking card, and, mainly, digging. When excavating, using the amount of knowledge the person has of that place, less time can bring greater odds of success (but not a guarantee), and the more time used, more pieces can be drawn from the bag. Regardless of how the time was used, the person in turn is always the one behind in time, which can, sometimes, make the same player have multiple turns in a row.

This is the way the game develops, with the player vyiing for the cards in Europe, which will give them better excavations, trying to make the best routes, and deciding how much to risk in the digging sites - as, the sooner someone goes in one, better are the chances of taking a valuable artifact (but, with less acquired knowledge, more time will be needed to draw more tokens), and the more of these are removed, harder it its to find the good pieces.

Thus, [thing=30869][/thing] becomes a race against time - players want to have the better possible odds before going dig, but this opens windows of opportunities for the others that accept the risk, and if this pays off, the odds of finding the artifacts get lower. Therefore, a good use of time, accepting some risk and calculating the probabilities, are the demands of [thing=30869][/thing] - a game of easy pace that doesn't demand much, but offers a nice amount of varied decisions and lots of chances for opportunity.

Luck is heavily present, still [thing=30869][/thing] soften it through players decisions, and gives use for any type of artifact, regardless of their points value, with the Expositions, along side the points for having the most in each type of specific knowledge. This way, luck is united with the core idea of [thing=30869][/thing] above which the theme and mechanism work and make sense - its a field with few certainties and lots of trial and error; the point, in the end, is not to waste precious time doubing yourself. Recommended.
