=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Thebes is a game of competitive archeology. Players are archaeologists who must travel around Europe, northern Africa, and the Middle East to acquire knowledge about five ancient civilizations -- the Greeks, the Cretans, the Egyptians, the Palestinians, and the Mesopotamians -- and then must use this knowledge to excavate historical sites in the areas of these civilizations. Through the course of the game, expositions are revealed, and an archaeologist who has treasures from the requisite civilizations may claim the prize (this is a change from the first edition's handling of exhibitions). The archaeologist who learns the most about the civilizations, claims the greatest-valued artifacts, and collects the most exhibitions will win out over his or her colleagues.
The key element to the game is that it is played out over a period of two (or three) years, and each action a player performs takes a certain amount of time -- traveling is a week between cities, gathering knowledge takes time for the level of the knowledge, and actually digging at a cultural site takes time to yield a certain number of artifact tiles. The game uses a novel mechanism to keep track of this. There is a track of 52 spaces around the outside of the board. Each time a player moves and takes an action, he or she moves their player token forward in time. Players take turns based on being the one who is furthest back in "time." So, a player can go to an excavation site and spend 10 weeks digging for artifacts, but that will also mean that the other players will likely be taking several actions in the interim while that player waits for the "time" to catch up.
In addition, the artifact tiles for each civilization are drawn from a bag that also contains dirt. When a player excavates a site, that player pulls tiles from the bag, but some may only be worthless dirt instead of valuable treasure. That dirt is then returned to the bag, making the first draw more likely to provide useful tiles.

RATING: 4
People say this is thematic, but I disagree.  And even if it might be thematic, that doesn't meant that something that is realistic translates into an interesting game, especially in terms of replayability.

The thing is, archaeologists don't keep going back to a dig site over and over.  They search for a spot to dig and come up with perhaps a lot of dead ends.  But then they find and site and they dig and until they think they're finished.  At no time does dirt/material go back into the site for someone else to then come along and get what was missed.  Maybe this was how it was done a hundred years ago, I don't know.

The result is a rather bland experience.  And I counter that even if it is enjoyable, it certainly can't be replicated over and over.  There are only so many times you can do this mechanic before it's going to lose its charm.  

Strongly prefer the card version which, counter to those who praise this edition while criticizing that one, is indeed more of a game.  Neither is deep by any stretch, but the card version actually has some real decisions and is not nearly as much at the whim of drawing blanks like the board game version.

RATING: 7.5
Very nice family weight game.  I know this is a negative for some people, but the person who plays the best doesn't always come out on top, and I like that in a game.  The new/inexperienced player has a shot at winning unless they just completely screw things up.  Plus, even if they lose, they can shrug it off because it's easy for them to convince themselves that if only their luck had been a little better, they could have won.

We previously owned Thebes: The Tomb Raiders (card game), and I like this original implementation better.  In streamlining it down into a card game, I feel like they took some of the soul of the game with it.  I much prefer "digging" through the bags rather than in decks of cards and I like the feeling of traveling across the map to acquire knowledge or go to dig sites.

RATING: 5
I really like the game mechanisms here.  The use of time as a currency is neat, and the way that it works with turn order means that you can coordinate your moves so that you can complete a chain of events on your turn.  Unfortunately, the game is managed by two random factors -- the card draw and the tile pull.  Managing your resources can help alleviate some of that, and yes, the debris tiles add to the theme, but it's frustrating to see your three opponents draw multiple huge artifacts from their draws, and then you draw the big goose egg on yours.

Also, I'm a little concerned with some players becoming so familiar with the cards in the deck that they know how to plan for exhibitions, or know which cities they should visit to get the cards they want.  I don't think this game brings that much depth to the table, but I can certainly see the hardcore gamers exploiting that aspect of the game.

Still, it's interesting enough and short enough that I will play it again.  It might even get better, now that I know how to manage my turns.

1 play: 3 players
2 plays: 4 players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A lot of what happens in Thebes is luck of the draw, which many people won't like. Normally I'd share that opinion, but so much of what happens is both mechanically clever and thematically appropriate that I just don't care. The primary action is drawing cards, for "study". The more knowledge the card gives you, the more time it takes you to learn it. Pick a deep tome and your opponents will take three or four turns before you get to go again.

Of course, having a boatload of knowledge about the area and the historical era you are searching for provides you with a huge advantage when it is time to go digging. Again, the game thematically shines here. You reach into a bag and pull chits, half of which are just empty dirt. When you pull dirt, it goes right back into the bag. The more an area is worked, the harder it becomes to find something valuable. It's an ingenious abstraction of archaeology...and one you will often curse when you spend 10 weeks pulling 8 chits and only managing to get three fairly worthless artifacts.

So yeah, there is a lot of luck. But it feels completely appropriate to the subject matter. Plus, it's just flat out hilarious when someone strikes out and equally as exciting when someone snags a high value trophy on a one week dig. I think this is one of those rare games that lets the fun out weigh the game's strategic shortcomings.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Thebes is a game of competitive archeology. Players are archaeologists who must travel around Europe to acquire knowledge about five ancient civilizations and then must use this knowledge to excavate historical sites in the areas of these civilizations. Through the course of the game, expositions are revealed, and an archaeologist who has treasures from the requisite civilizations may claim the prize. The archaeologist who learns the most about the civilizations, claims the greatest-valued artifacts, and collects the most exhibitions will win out over his or her colleagues.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I love this game! You represent archeological diggers in Europe. Players spend the first turns travelling between cities in Europe, amassing knowledge about the dig sites. Each turn takes a number of weeks to perform, and it's always the player who's last on the time line who gets to go. This is a really interesting mechanism.
When playeers have sufficient knowledge for a special dig site, they go there and decide how many weeks to spend digging, and then draw a certain number of treasure chits from a cloth bag representing the dig site. The more knowledge you have, and the more weeks you spend, the more chits you can draw. Treasures are worth points.
Apart from finding treasures, players can earn points holding conferences, or holding exhibits (this requires you to have a certain combination of found treasures).
The mix between the turn system, the cloth bag digging and the different ways to get points makes up for a real fantastic game.
